CHAIR LEE: Good morning.
PRESENTATION OF ORAL TESTIMONY
MS. KEISA LU:
Good morning, everyone. Mahalo, my name is Keisa Lu and I'm here to speak on
agenda item "E. RECOGNIZING HUNGER AND HOMELESSNESS AWARENESS
WEEK". Apologies for not having a, a completely written out thing like I normally do. I
did submit written testimony on this agenda item.  Isupport this with reservation. As
we continue to move. I'm asking that as we continue to move towards holistic solutions.
Now we operate a housing first model, but it does feel a lot more like housing only.
The support is focused on housing, which of course right now is inadequate. For
example, when I spoke with Ka Hale A Ke Ola, they said that they have a 90-day
program. And when I asked them if that was sufficient to get someone through the
program and into permanent housing they said no, that takes really an average about
six months. So when we talk about our housing first model, there's I'm giving you one
example how it's not adequate.
But the other thing that we're missing is the understanding that as humans we need to
be able to move out of traumatic situations and understand that houselessness and
hunger is traumatic. And it requires a level of emotional intelligence, emotional safety,
compassion, aloha, and trauma-informed decisions, which again are lacking currently.
Now watching the interactions between the residents and our governing bodies and
whoever else is involved, I see that and seeing that we fund, and see what we fund
and how we fund services. I believe it's not enough to have services available the way
that we and the way that we offer those services is not helping enough.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 5
So as we recognize that this is an issue here on, in our County, I am going to ask our
entire community, everyone on this call, the Council, the Mayor, and everyone who
lives in Maui County to refuse, to stop using the phrase "refuser of services" as a way
or an excuse not to care about our community. Because it's incredibly inaccurate. It
places the blame on the victims when clearly there are a lot of things that get in the
way of being able to take services, and when those services are provided, it's always,
it's not always enough.
I urge leadership to stop spreading that phrase, to stop using that when you talk about
our houseless community in the media or with community members. Stop using that.
Stop. They are not jackasses. They are not people who are refusing water that's being
given to them. The path is difficult. The water is not there. Stop.
CHAIR LEE: Can you.
MS. LU: I'm going to leave, I'm going to leave this now. Yes, absolutely. I urge our community
and leadership to start recognizing that just providing shelter is not enough. That these
are humans. They are ours. They are part of our community. Mahalo for your time.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you.
Member Johnson, you have a question?
COUNCILMEMBER JOHNSON: Yes, thank you. Chair. Good morning, Ms. Lu. And I just
want to appreciate you for all your tireless efforts to help those folks that we just were,
you were speaking on. So my clarifying question is you mentioned that we can do
better with a holistic approach. Can you speak on that? Like what specifically do you
mean as far as a more holistic approach when it comes to helping people who are
houseless.
MS. LU: It's systematic or systemic issue. One of the things that we can work on is
diversifying the services that are being provided. Especially when it comes to the
housing, you know, we're talking about housing first or shelter. Right now we have
very limited shelters. They aren't 24-hours. They are limited spaces. You know, they
aren't always helpful for what is needed. So on a larger scale, I would suggest
managed encampments.
Because one of the things that happens, especially when those sweeps happen, or
clean-ups happen. Manage, we don't, the services, service providers don't have a way
to contact the people they are trying to help. So every time that happens it starts over
again, and we have to find them again. And so if we have managed encampments,
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 6
we at least have a space where we can start that process and continue to keep those
touch points because the other thing I would say is that the touch points need to
happen, and they need to happen compassionately. They need to happen with people
who are trauma-informed, and do, not only are they informed, but they actually act in
that way. It's one thing to just take the training, it's another thing to actually do that
type of work. So, you know, in order to do all that, you have to do that.
So the other thing 1 would also say is to stop moving people, because ail we’re doing
is creating more work on our already strained resources. It's expensive and it doesn't
help combat the situation that we currently have. So those are a few ideas that I would
think would help.
COUNCILMEMBER JOHNSON: Thank you so much, Ms. Lu. Thank you, Chair. I have no
further questions.
CHAIR LEE; Any more questions, Members? If not, thank you very much, Ms. Lu.
Mr. Clerk, next testifier.
DEPUTY COUNTY CLERK; The last testifier we have signed up to testify on ceremonial
resolutions is Jordan Hocker.
MS. JORDAN HOCKER;
Hello, Council and Members. Aloha mai Council Chair and Vice-Chair Rawlins-
Fernandez. My name is Jordan Hocker, I'm here on my own time and my own behalf.
I'd like to begin with gratitude for this resolution. I'd like to thank Gabe Johnson for this.
I have truly seen a cohesiveness between this resolution and the work that you and
your office are doing. So 1 really support that, and I support this resolution.
I do need to state for consideration for this body, the amount of unpaid hours that myself
and other unpaid volunteers are putting in the time away from our families, the times
out of our routine that we are putting in to figure out where we are failing our
unsheltered residents. Keisa does not get paid for the forty plus hours a week that she
does. I don't get paid for figuring out where the holes are.
I really am here to urge all Members of this government and this body to really, to be
listening to address the biases that prevent care from actually reaching our unsheltered
members. I really truly worry about lip service with a . . . addressing hunger and
homelessness that doesn't follow through with respect for our unsheltered residents.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 7
I  have been before this body multiple times to share my own experience with
homelessness and to ask you folks at what point in my experience of chronic illness
and not being able to work and thus facing eviction at an earlier point in my life, was I
not worthy of respect or was I unable to reach your hearts? Was it with . . . and I ask
these questions, was it when the eviction notice happened, was it when I needed to
sleep in my car, was it when I was living out of my car.
The other day I was sitting in a parking lot in Makawao waiting for a doctor's
appointment, and I saw MPD be called for an unsheltered resident. And they called
one of the outreach services, which I'm sure folks are familiar with. There’s really only
one funded entity that has outreach funds. And I listened to that process, and I listened
to her speak with him.
And you know I come from a background of hospitality, I worked and managed
restaurants for about a decade. And when someone would come into my restaurant, i
had things to offer them, right, and I was happy that they were there, because it was
my job to make sure that they knew everything we offered, and they had the best
experience possible.
And so as I'm sitting there listening to this, I don't hear an offering of services. I don't
hear, you know, someone, this person said this is what I need, and they said well unless
you take our whole program, we can't help you with this. And the person didn't want
to move down to Kahului, because it's not as safe there to be out of the studio. They
wanted to remain Upcountry, because it's safer .  ..  And I'll just, I have two more
sentences.
In that experience, I realized this isn't catered towards them. It's catered towards the
establishments that are providing services and it's catered towards the Federal funding
and the funding that the County is doing. We need  anew model that caters towards
people, that delivers them dignity and respect and reminds them that we are all
humans, and it doesn't matter whether we're struggling or not? We are on equal
playing-field. So I support this resolution, thank you folks so much today for letting me
speak.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. Any questions for Ms. Mocker? If not, thank you very much.

for over 20 years, like an actual dedicated crisis that's just been acknowledged by the
Department of Justice. And the fact that it is not named, and we are still afraid to say
mental illness is something that adds to the stigma, and it does not bring forward
healing and healthy solutions. And I think it gets ignored.
And I really want to put a very, very strong push in to recognize how many times I've
been in doctor's offices with individuals when they got their diagnoses. And whether
it's cancer, diabetes, or schizophrenia, there's always been a great relief when an
illness is named, because now you can actually treat it. And although it may be
something that is difficult and uncomfortable and nobody wants to hear the word
schizophrenia, I think it's probably the meanest disease on the planet. At least it
becomes treatable and until we acknowledge and put this forward, we will always
stumble.

COUNCILMEMBER SINENCI:
SECOND.

(The resolution was read in its entirety.)
CHAIR LEE: Any discussion?
Member Johnson.
COUNCILMEMBER JOHNSON: Thank you. Chair and thank you, Mr. Deputy Clerk.
Domestic violence, divorce, paternity, and custody issues affect many individuals and
families in Maui County. Established in 2020 Family Court Awareness Month is an
opportunity for survivors of domestic violence, or post-separation abuse, to amplify
their voices while raising awareness of shortcomings in the family court system.
By supporting the Family Court Awareness Month, Maui County would join 200 other
cities and counties nationwide to stand with local and national advocacy groups
highlighting the importance of protecting children and other vulnerable individuals in
the family court system. 1 ask for the Members full support of this resolution. Thank
you, Chair.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 13
CHAIR LEE: Thank you, any more discussion Members? If not, all those in favor of adopting
the resolution, please raise your hand and say "aye".
AYES: COUNCILMEMBERS JOHNSON. KAMA, KING,
MOLINA, PALTIN, SINENCI, SUGIMURA,
VICE-CHAIR RAWLINS-FERNANDEZ, AND
CHAIR LEE.
NOES: NONE.
CHAIR LEE: Nine "ayes", zero "noes"; motion carries. Resolution adopted.
Member Johnson.
COUNCILMEMBER JOHNSON: Chair, I would like to recognize Breana Pakele, the TRO
Program Coordinator at Women Helping Women to receive and speak to this
resolution. Mahalo.
CHAIR LEE: Ms. Pakele.
MS. BREANA PAKELE: Hi, good morning, Councilmembers and everyone present today.
My name is Breana Pakele, I'm the Temporary Restraining Order Coordinator at
Women Helping Women and I have been with the agency for about six years or so. I
would like to express my gratitude to the Maui County Council for recognizing hunger
and homelessness week, as well as deeming November as Family Court Awareness
Month.
During the shutdown in March of 2020, the Second Circuit Court, Women Helping
Women, and other agencies throughout Maui County have made some outside the box
changes that not only eliminated interruption of services, but created safer, more
efficient means for victims to obtain protective orders. Through conversation, this
partnership was able to allow TRO petitions to be faxed to the courthouse, removing
that barrier of a victim having to physically go there and process documents that way.
And they were also able to appear via Webex for their court hearings, which eliminates
that fear of being present within the courtroom.
Many see additional challenges through custody and divorce hearings with much more
support is needed. This is where great ideas are needed for safety and support for our
victims and especially for the children that continue to live in fear, despite all of these
drawn-out custody hearings. The voices of our children need to be heard and truly
listened to. And there needs to be a more deeper understanding of domestic violence
and how its present in the system. Having judges and court staff and advocates who
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 14
truly understand the dynamics of domestic violence, and the unseen effects our
systems play on victims is vital to their success.
Last year in 2020 our agency had processed 439 TROs from January to December,
and that number continues to grow larger each year. Violence is ever changing, and
ongoing education is a huge need for everybody. For many victims court isn't the end
of their journey, many need additional ongoing support to help them process and heal
from abuse, to regain their sense of self and lead better lives.
Having the programs here at Women Helping Women include court advocacy and
support allows us as advocates to support and guide these victims through the difficult
court process. The TROs that are filed, which can be as many know intimidating and
a very fearful thing to go through alone. As many know, the court system is often used
as another tool for the abuser to continue to maintain power and control over their
victim. It's important that these types of cases are recognized and treated in a manner
different from any ordinary custody or divorce case.
Our agency is very, very proud to be able to provide the assistance and support that
we do to all the victims of domestic violence through TRO processing, court advocacy,
shelter services, assistance through our transitions program and more. And I just
wanted to say thank you, Councilmembers, for highlighting the importance of two very
critical issues and systems, and while domestic violence continues to be a pandemic
in itself, we are hopeful and we continue to, you know, do our work in Maui County and
for our victims. That's alt. Mahalo.
CHAIR LEE; Thank you, Ms. Pakele. Any questions?
Member Sugimura.
COUNCILMEMBER SUGIMURA: Fantastic, impressive. That's a lot of TRO's and I think this

hidden solutions. So once the pandemic and this, the courts go back in session, would
they be allowed to continue to have online hearings with the, with the courts or will it
go back to normal in-person. Do you know?
MS. PAKELE: I don't think they've gotten that far yet. But as of now they are planning on
continuing for some time.
COUNCILMEMBER SUGIMURA: That's good. Tasha Kama has an item that maybe it could

community settings that makes it easier for people. So I wonder if this could be added

and the shortage of affordable housing in Maui County. These issues have only been

Hunger and Homeless /s/cj Awareness Week, the County would join a national call to

Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 20
This is a systemic problem and at one point, the system was aina momona, shared
abundance. The system that we're in now allows for wealth inequality. The fact that
hunger, homelessness, and poverty in the wealthiest country, while billionaires exist is
a failure of systems. The continuation of treating this as an issue of a failure of the
individual or thinking that charity is the answer is just complacency on our part and
allowing this system to continue.
So I mahalo again Member Johnson, and mahalo to all the Members on this Council
for all of your work for addressing this systemic problem at the core. And I hope that
other elected officials will see this problem as  asystemic problem and fix this at the
core and not just address the symptoms. Mahalo, Chair.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. Vice-Chair. I, too, want to thank you, say a thank you to Member
Johnson for offering this wonderful resolution and for all the comments made by all of
the Members. I agree that this is a systemic problem. However, unfortunately the
Council doesn't have control over the entire system.
So for what we do have control over, last budget session, the Council wisely, the
Budget Committee wisely introduced the initiative that allows for nonprofits, especially
new nonprofits to have an advance instead of having to be subject to the total
reimbursement system, which makes it really hard for any new nonprofit.
And I think maybe in the upcoming session, we need to really identify some very good
umbrella nonprofit agencies, because they can do the paperwork. They know how to
handle the red tape, unlike the, the people in the field, you know? They're, they're the
ones that need to be free to do the work and not be stymied and, and held back by
paperwork, which other nonprofits could probably handle.
So 1 know we all want to do more, and I think we will, especially in the budget

MS. DEB LYNCH: First of all, well, thank you all so much for all your hard work that you do.
Thank you, Member Johnson, you're really a great asset and thank you all
Councilmembers because I know you are all very concerned about the issues of the
unsheltered people here.
Most of you probably ail know who A Cup of Cold Water, we've been around for about
seven years, and we are 100 percent volunteer and accept no government money. We
go out a few times a week, the pandemic of course limited some of our activities at
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 22
times. And we bring water, food, hygiene, first aid, clothes, blankets, towels, a lot of
compassion directly to the people, unsheltered people, we look for them.
In listening to your numbers, so far this year even with the times when we had to
suspend our runs, we've served almost 2,200 different individuals. Some of them are
the same individuals, yes. But in 2020 our numbers were just over 4,000. So there
are many people out there. Maui people that are unsheltered hide a lot so when the
point count, Point Count In Time happens there are times that they don't catch
everybody.
One thing that A Cup of Cold Water does besides bringing things is we are a great
conveyor of information to the unsheltered and likewise getting information back from
them on what their concerns are. An example was when the Salvation Army had the
vaccination clinic, we were able to share that information with the unsheltered as they
wanted to go get vaccinated.
We hear back from people who are looking for shelter, saying yes, that unfortunately
with some of the shelter placements with their funding they are restricted with how they
can accept people with certain situations going on in their lives. And that does make it
very difficult for some of the people who are unsheltered who would like to become
sheltered, and they can't get in because of restrictions.
So A Cup of Cold Water as in, as in like Maui Rescue Mission, we're sort of the bridge
between living on the streets and opportunities for people to get services and shelter.
They call us dusty boots on the ground. The other thing that we like to do at Cup of
Cold Water is invite people to come out on one of our runs. They're not long, they're
just a few hours. Come see the people, meet them. See what they need. We also
would love to have other organizations and ministries come as guest riders also, if they
have a service or something that they would like to offer to the unsheltered this way
they get to meet them, they get to have a real conversation with them, really talk. So
again, I want to thank you all so much for passing this resolution.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you. And we have Mr., who's next?
COUNCiLMEMBER JOHNSON: Mr. Hansen.
CHAIR LEE: Mr. Hansen. Mr. Hansen.
MR. SCOTT HANSEN: Yes, aloha, good morning. Thank you. Council. I appreciate the
invite. I have spoken to you guys before, but 1 just wanted to touch on a couple
important aspects of Maui Rescue Mission. We are mobile, we bring as you probably
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 23
remember a trailer with shower and laundry capabilities to four different locations four
days a week. And we're mobile so that we can get to people that can't get to us.
There's some issues that most homeless people encounter either they have had
trauma in the past, they have struggles with mental health issues. Many of them have
transportation issues, they can't get to the resources that they need to get to. And then
there's also issues with their pride, asking for help. They feel like they can dig
themselves out of the hole that they're in by themselves sometimes and they just need
a friend to come along and say, you know, you need a little bit of help and we're here
to help you. So that's why we're mobile.
We realize that many people can't get to the resources they need to, need to get to.
So what we've done is, we've been able to collaborate with a lot of other agencies, and
individuals, some volunteers that are very dedicated, and we've created a mobile
resource center in four different locations a week that they can get to. We're really big
into collaborating, cause we can't do it all. So we have Malama I Ke Ola Health Center,
for example, they come out. We are not health professionals, we can't offer health
insurance, but they can. So that's why we create the environment to allow the
collaborations to occur. And we're willing to collaborate with anybody. We appreciate
the collaboration of the, the County as well.
So what makes it so helpful and, and so successful is that the resources are right there.
So even though someone comes, thinking they only need a shower, we invite them to
also sit down and maybe get SNAP benefits if they don't have it? Or get help with their
EBT, or just talk to us, and discuss what issues they are having. Cause the other
aspect of what we do is we try to fill in any gaps we can possibly fill in, which means
that we don't have very many boundaries of how we can help people.
And we do it with the utmost dignity and respect. So when people come onto the
property wherever we are set up, the first thing we do is we try to notice them coming
and we welcome them. We say hi, how are you doing? What can we help you with
today? What do you need help with? If they say nothing, if they just are checking it
out, then that's fine.
One of the other things that has made our outreaches so inviting is we've been able to
collaborate with different, mostly volunteers, but other agencies that will provide food.
So we serve during lunchtime, and we noticed in the past some of our guests would
leave to go get lunch, and that makes it harder for us to build a relationship with them,
it makes it harder us, for us to get the resource help to them that they need. They
might be on the list for SNAP benefits, and they need to get lunch and then they miss
their opportunity. So that has provided a great way to keep them onsite that we can
help them.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 24
So I just want, you know, a couple of the testifiers mentioned the dignity piece, and not
just walking up to people and saying, you know, do you need shelter, I can help you
get shelter. I just want to give you a quick example of why that doesn't, doesn't work.
We had a gentleman who recently became homeless, was living in his car. And our,
one of our outreach workers . . . approached him basically every week, at least once a
week, a couple times a week. She would go to where he parked his car and she would
talk to him and say, hey, we can help you, come to the shower, get, get a shower, get
your clothes washed, there are resources. It took almost six months before he finally
was able to let down his guard and convince himself to come for help. Since that, he
got his car stolen, he got his phone stolen. His car had all his things in it. He got lower
and lower and lower in the situation.
We've come to find out he is a veteran. So we hooked him up with veteran services.
He has received our compassion and our help, and he went from a very angry man to
now he is a very grateful man and loves to come to tell us how grateful he is for us.
We, he's applied for, he might even have gotten his HUD voucher so he's in line to get
housing. And we've just been able to help him with so many resources. And it's
because we took the time over and over to say hey, we're here to help. We love you.
we care about you, you're important, and when you're ready for help, we're here.
Rather than just say, we have help, do you want it? And when he said no, we just left
him and never approached him again.
They're humans, the people we deal with are humans. So we create a very, very open
door. Anyone can receive our services without giving us their actual name if they don't
want to until it's time to actually apply for something where it makes sense that they
give us their name. And we love to collaborate. So it starts with compassion,
collaboration, and that, we have seen leads to success. We've seen many people get
back up on their feet. We've sent people to the mainland to reconnect with family or to
get into a rescue mission or something on the mainland to help them. So those two
items, I think if we can adopt them across the board, 1 think we'd see a lot of change
happening. Thank you so much for, for listening to my speech.
CHAIR LEE: Thank you, Mr. Hansen. Members, any questions? If not, thank you very much.

PRESENTATION OF ORAL TESTIMONY
MS. KEISA LU [testifying on Committee Report No. 21-117 and Bill No. 135 (2021)1:
Good morning. Aloha, my name is Keisa Lu and I'm here to testify on two agenda
items today. Agenda item "M.", Bill 135, and agenda item "K." CR 21-117. And I'll start
with Bill 135 really quickly. 1 just wanted to say mahalo to Councilmember King for
bringing this forward and for the rest of the Council for pushing it forward. And I
apologize for not being able to stay on top of this particular bill. I have been a little bit
preoccupied, but I am in absolute in full support of this. I love that it's changed as well,
to have a little bit more stringent things on here for us. So I'm very happy, and I just
want to mahalo everyone here for, for pushing that forward. And I am, of course, in full
support.
So the other one that 1 will be talking about a little bit, again I didn't have time to put
together a written testimony for me to say right now. So it is going to be on the fly
CR 21-17 [sicj. You know, I, again, am in support of this. This resolution 1 understand
that it is aspirational in nature, but again it's part of that work of moving forward,
changing mindsets about what adequate housing means, what, what that looks like
here on Maui and what we need to do to help move things forward. A couple of things
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 28
that I just wanted to highlight that the community, the committee noted which was the
right to adequate housing means not only a physical dwelling but housing that is
affordable, habitable, accessible, culturally appropriate, and secure. And that's a really
important designation that we need here on our island.
I am happy that it's expanded to include individuals dealing with mental health. What
I've seen is that I have yet to meet a houseless individual who doesn't struggle with
some type of mental or physical health, and it is very much a part of this population.
So acknowledging that like Lisa Darcy said is incredibly helpful in terms of making sure
those services are provided. So I mahalo for too.
And I also want to thank you for pointing out that there's a clear priority now. And that
when we move forward, that it is responsive and effective. And this is really important,
because the reality is we are in a crisis. And 1 want to highlight this one thing in
particular, why, why it is a crisis. We do have people dying in the streets on Maui
especially, but in Maui County.
And 1 want to tell you two stories before 1 get off today. The first one is, you know, in
my written testimony I explained how I am helping  ahouseless individual, and how I
needed to drop them off on the streets in order for them to qualify for homeless
verification form even though it was well-known they were from Amala and that they
were houseless. That day that I did that, they had just found out that one of their friends
who was also houseless had passed on the streets. And I was, had a lot of feelings of
guilt at that time, because I had to drop them at  atime of trauma.
The second thing I want to say is that I personally know of somebody who passed,
again, on the streets, who was from Amala. This person showed up at the sweep or
cleanup with, what's it called, those bracelets, the hospital bracelets. And 1 recognized
them immediately and 1 said oh what are you doing here? And they said well I was
worried that my stuff was going to get taken, so I'm here. So they checked themselves
out early from the hospital, to go check on their things at Amala. And it wasn't long
after that they were admitted to the hospital again, after attempting to get into a shelter.
You know, they weren't able to do that. They were, had to go into, to the hospital
because they had gotten so sick, and they ended up passing.
So when we talk about the fact that this is a crisis, I need you to understand that we
are losing our people because of the way that we're doing things right now. And I'm
going to say it one more time, because I need you to hear it. We are losing our people,
good people, because of our system right now. So, as much as I am happy that this is
moving forward, I need you to continue to recognize ... we have to push forward,
because it is a crisis. Mahalo. And one last thing is 1 did hear Chair Lee say that you

MR. JEFF BAGSHAW ftestifvina on Bill No. 135 (2021)1:
Aloha, Maui County Council. Mahaio for having the courage to address the issues in
Bill 135, which I am in support of. My name is Jeff Bagshaw. I am in favor because
this bill will remove environmentally hazardous materials that have been plaguing our
island for decades. It does what we have been unable to do with thousands of hours
of outreach and education. It recognizes that relying on consumers to make the right
choices has not been enough, especially in the face of advertising campaigns and
green-washing marketing. It stops the problem where it begins, in the store. It's very
difficult to correct the issue when people arrive at the beach. And that only reaches a
minority through that education. And it, it makes it clear that we're not fooled, that
reformulating and renaming a compound does not make it safe for the environment.
This bill also, it does not only protect our reefs, but it protects our food sources for our
community. This is an issue of environmental justice because one consumer's choice
of using toxic sun protection products should not override the rights of others to foods
without benzines.
There may be dermatologists who have a vested interest in the industry or make their
own sun care products defending chemical sunscreens. But a credible dermatologist
who do not have a interest in the industry will tell you that mineral products are actually
more effective at protecting against UVA and UVB rays.
Between 2009 and 2018 the National Cancer Institute recorded a rise in skin cancer
rates of 1.4 percent annually. Yet these products have been on the market since 1978.
So if they are so effective, why are skin cancer rates still rising?
They may claim that people want, people need more choices, they may not use mineral
sun blocks because they are pasty looking. And there's even been claims that people
have sensitivity to it. Zinc oxide is used to treat diaper rash. So the number of people
who may have sensitivity to mineral sun blocks is very small.

I'd like the industry to explain why, why people, we hear about numbers that people
may get skin cancer and yet we don't hear numbers from the industry about how many
people are affected by second hand benzine contamination or from these products
themselves. We don't know what the costs are to our shoreline protection, our food
resources, when we talk about loss of sales in the stores. I think those should be
compared.
ni just say that I'll just close by saying that we have always had choices that have

DR. GEORGE MARTIN [testifying on Bill No. 135 (2021)1:
Mahalo, Chair Lee and distinguished Members of the Council. I hope you can hear me
well. It's good to see familiar faces. I've lived on Maui for 32 years and I want to
provide testimony in opposition to Bill 135, which prohibits the sale, distribution, and
even use of non-mineral sunscreens without prescription.
So as you know I'm George Martin. I'm American Board certified in both dermatology
and internal medicine, and practice dermatology in Kihei, where I practiced for
32 years. I am representing two bodies of dermatologists who are all in favor of this
position: the Hawaii Dermatologic Society and the Academy, the American Academy
of Dermatology Association. 1 am not a paid lobbyist.
I’m a very concerned citizen, waterman who loves the ocean, loves the reef and I'm
also treat thousands of skin cancers. So as I mentioned, I practice dermatology since
1989. I've treated over 20,000 patients and 30,000 skin cancer. My wife and I and
children have lived on Maui, we've taught our children the beauty of the ocean and the
reefs, we fight for its safety.
I'm also President and founding member of DASIL International Society of
Dermatologist, and I lecture all over the world on skin cancer and photo protection on
the use of sunscreens. I've also been involved since 1986 on sunscreen research and
development. I've developed reef-safe sunscreen products that will launch in 2022,
80 percent of them are mineral and I understand the value of mineral sunscreens. I
also understand the value of chemical sunscreens which outperform mineral
sunscreens and where they fit.
Here is the crux of the matter, if, if mineral sunscreens worked as well as chemical
sunscreens, we would have no chemical sunscreens. With one exception when you

wear a mineral sunscreen and you apply proper amounts, you kind of look like Marcel

Beginning with Bill 118 on the ‘aina kupuna land, we support this bill to take care of our

paradise. We appreciate some of the thoughts that we raised were included in this bill
and taken into account with the definition for descendants added, and the date revised
from 80 years rolling, so that it could be rolling forward for future generations. So we
deeply appreciate those considerations.

these legitimate businesses. So we want to, you know, acknowledge that there is some

Planning, you know, Department recommendations. However, we do feel that again

And I was listening to the testimony earlier where it was said that this bill is going to
allow for economic diversification and for job changes. And economic development
takes time, it takes significant investment. We all understood during COVID how
dependent we are on the visitor industry, and we knew that going in before COVID.
And work was starting to change in terms of looking at new ways to better manage our
visitor industry and to further economic development. But COVID really was a, a strong
slap that said we really need to do more.
But economic development doesn't happen overnight. It takes significant investment,
it. . . targeted industries that also well support our environment and our quality of life.
So we really need to have processes that allow flexibility, and not hamper our goals
and ... at a time when we are still very dependent on this industry that supports so
many industries. And we feel that really, there are so many things that could be done
right now, really investing in the destination management action plan that has been put
forward that we work with the industry and work to better manage it, but also work in
ways that we continue to allow appropriate renovations and expansions.
We also feel that, again, moratoriums are very inflexible, and if you're going to go
forward with the moratorium there should be a strong rationale with data that illustrates
the best solution, and that, that really the moratorium is the only way to move forward,
and we haven't seen that. And ultimately this is not going to stop visitors from traveling
to Maui. We believe it will have negative impacts such as reduced industry revenue,
reduced construction revenue, and increased costs per guests. Which could potentially
lead to stays in illegal units or people going elsewhere at a time when many industries
are counting on our visitor industry.
So for these reasons we ask that you defer this until compelling evidence is available,
till we've brought in leading economists to talk about the, some of the potential negative
impacts that might occur, and until we can address some of the key things that we need

MR. ALBERT PEREZ. MAUI TOMORROW FOUNDATION (testifying on Committee Report
No. 21-130):
Sorry, you surprised me. I thought there was one more before me. Anyway, I'm Albert
Perez and I’m testifying on the visitor accommodations moratorium. You know, local
resident sentiment about the visitor industry has fallen to an all-time low and our
overdependence on tourism leaves us at-risk from future economic downturns as well.
This is a problem that's continuing to grow, and we need to stop making it worse. We
need to pause continued expansion of the already overbuilt tourism industry. The
proposal is to pause further development for two years or when solutions
recommended by the Temporary Investigative Group are passed by the Council, and
my understanding is that that group is already close to finishing its work.
Some people say that the moratorium would lead to more illegal vacation rentals, but
that assumes a lack of enforcement and that's not acceptable policy for the County. If
tourist accommodations are limited and we have strong enforcement, then Maui will
return to being a premiere destination, and existing accommodations will be more
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 63
profitable with more benefits, more jobs, and a better quality of life for residents. Let's
be consistent with the Maui island Plan. This pause is a first step. There's broad public
support.
I would like to suggest that Dick Mayer's proposed amendment be adopted to make
sure that this ordinance applies to any application that has not received final approval.
I understand that there are some hotels that are ready to submit their applications. So
we don't want them slipping in. And anyway, with that amendment, I would hope that
you would support this proposed ordinance. Mahalo.

previous years, Section 13C of the HSAC bylaws requires all four county councils to

Legislature. Of the seven legislative proposals our County submitted, four were
deemed appropriate by the HSAC Executive Committee to be included in the HSAC
Legislative Package. Those proposals that were not included were instead supported
as legislative priorities, meaning that the HSAC Executive Committee will provide
testimony in support of similar legislation.

Chock, Exhibit "A", which also includes all the legislative package bills, i ask the
Members full support of this resolution and invite Councilmember King to provide
additional comments as she would like to.

the recommendations in Committee Report 21-117.
Discussion, Member Johnson.
COUNCILMEMBER JOHNSON; Thank you, Chair. The purpose of the proposed resolution
is to establish a right to housing for all Maui County residents as a central component
to a Comprehensive Affordable Housing Plan and our objective as a County to meet
the housing needs of the Maui Nui community.
Your Committee discussed the resolution which is supported by international human
rights law and Countywide Policy Plan. It affirms  aright to an affordable, habitable,
accessible, culturally appropriate, secure, and safe residence. It also recognizes that
adequate housing enhances safety, stability, economic productivity, civic participation,
overall quality of life for everyone.
The resolution requires the Council and the County to prioritize and provide adequate
inventory of housing and shelter options, use all available resources to progressively
improve the housing situation in the County, and recognize, respect, and protect the
right of all residents living in security, peace, and dignity.
Your Committee voted 8-0 to recommend adoption of the resolution and agreed that
additional language was needed to include persons living with behavioral health issues
among those who should be given special consideration. I have addressed this
recommendation in the Amendment Summary Forms. So, I, 1 respectfully request the
Members support of the motion.

November 19, 2021
Page 97

COUNCILMEMBER KAMA: Thank you. Chair. I just want to share a little bit of history about
the Act itself. At the time of the passage of the Act, there was never, ever any funding
from the Federal, or even on Territorial side for homesteader. Not until 1983 did the
Department of Hawaiian Home Lands actually get funded from the State just for the
operations of that department. So, from the time it became a State in 1959 to '83, the
Department, who was operating with a zero budget.
So today, what we're getting is people are learning how to read the Act and the Trust,
with the eyes of Prince Kuhio. Anybody can read the Trust and read the Act. You
know. I've looked this. It's a desktop reference guide. So, I bought the Hawaiian
Homes Commission Act and I've had this for like 20 years. And it tells us the story of
how Prince Kuhio looked at what his people could do on our land.
But once other people get a hold of it and they misinterpret the thinking of native
people, then they end up going in a totally different direction. Only today, and I say
today that this 21st century, have we actually been able to look at that Trust and see
how that Trust has been so ill-managed and underfunded. And so, what we're trying
to do today is to bring things up to where they should have been in 1920. So, here we
are 101 years later trying to do the right thing, 100 years later.
So, this legislation is the beginning of getting the Native Hawaiian peoples onto their
lands and to doing what exactly what Prince Kuhio wanted, was to rehabilitate his
people. And homelessness was present in 1920 for the Hawaiians, cause they were
all off their lands. They were all living in the cities on . . . street.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 101
So, this is, to me, it’s going to be very helpful to our people, because the commercial
developments that we have in Kapolei, commercial developments on the Big Island,
the Prince Kuhio Shopping Center, it's all Hawaiian Home Lands. And those were
made so that they can develop revenue to put more Hawaiians on land. But this way,
it's more helpful that you don't have to be renting out your good lands for commercial
development. You could just go right straight to building homesteads for our native

Discussion, Vice-Chair Rawlins-Fernandez.
VICE-CHAIR RAWLINS-FERNANDEZ: Mahalo, Chair. Your Budget, Finance, and
Economic Development Committee met on November 4, 2021, to discuss three
proposed bills and one proposed resolution relating to the Acquisition of 1888 Wili Pa
Loop Building and Property. The proposed bills amend the Budget bill, Appendix C
bill, and the Bond Authorization bill to add funding and a project description under the
Department of Water Supply, Wailuku-Kahului Community Plan Area for the
Acquisition of 1888 Wili Pa Loop Building and Property in the amount of $4,020,000.
The resolution authorizes the acquisition of 29,712 square feet identified as tax map
key (2) 3-4-020-049, situated at Wailuku, for an approximate, oh sorry, for an amount
not to exceed $4 million, plus customary expenses.
The Department of Water Supply informed your Committee that approximately
30 Fiscal Division employees would be relocated from the fifth floor of the Kalana o
Maui Building to the new space. Nine employees in the Water Resources and Planning
Division would then be able to move out of their current rental space at One Main Plaza
and into Kalana o Maui. This move would also allow 25 Engineering Division
employees more office space in Kalana o Maui for improved social distancing.
Your Committee noted the Wili Pa Loop Building is already set up with teller windows
and a vault to accept payments, which would then free up daily customer traffic to
Kalana o Maui. The Department also said the new space contains a conference room
large enough for the Board of Water Supply to hold meetings.
Your Committee voted 7-0 to recommend passage of the proposed bills and adoption
of the proposed resolution.
Member Sugimura also scheduled an appointment to check out the conference room
and the building. And I'm excited to hear her report, if she was able to get that site visit
done. I respectfully ask the Members support of the motion. Mahalo, Chair.

previous meeting had asked for a site visit, but once we got new information, we

Discussion? Member Paltin.
COUNCILMEMBER PALTIN: Thank you, Chair. Your Planning and Sustainable Land Use
Committee, having met on October 21, 2021, makes reference to County
Communication 21-457, from the Planning Director, transmitting a proposed bill
entitled 'A BILL FOR AN ORDINANCE AMENDING SECTION 19.06.020, MAUI
COUNTY CODE, ADOPTING DIGITAL ZONING MAP (3) AS THE OFFICIAL ZONING
MAP FOR THE ISLAND OF MAUI".
Purpose of the proposed bill is to adopt digital zoning map (3) as the official zoning
map for the island of Maui. Map (3) includes two corrections to map (2) which was
adopted by the Council on November 13, 2019, Ordinance 5006.
Your Committee notes a digital zoning map provides a graphical portrayal of zoning
designations established by ordinance.

bill at its meeting of August 10, 2021 and voted unanimously to support the bill with
revisions.

recordation of the unilateral agreement, and filing of the communication.

Page 134
CHAIR LEE: Moved by Member Paltin, seconded by Vice-Chair Rawlins-Fernandez, to
approve the recommendations in Committee Report 21-130.
Discussion, Member Paltin.
COUNCILMEMBER PALTIN: Thank you, Chair. Your Planning and Sustainable Land Use
Committee, having met on November 3, 2021, makes reference to County
Communication 21-280, transmitting a proposed resolution "REFERRING TO THE
MAUI PLANNING COMMISSION A BILL TO ESTABLISH CHAPTERS 19.98 AND
20.41, MAU! COUNTY CODE, DECLARING A MORATORIUM ON NEW TRANSIENT
ACCOMMODATIONS ON MAUI".
The purpose ofthe proposed bill is to establish Chapters 19.98 and 20.41, Maui County
Code, for a moratorium to maintain the number of transient accommodations units for
a two-year period until the Council enacts legislation addressing the findings and
recommendations presented by a Budget, Finance, and Economic Development
Committee-established Tourism Management Temporary Investigative Group, or two
years from the enactment of the bill, whichever is sooner.
Your Committee notes the Maui Planning Commission, at its meeting of September
28, 2021, recommended approval ofthe proposed bill attached to Resolution 21-98,
with revisions.
Your Committee received numerous written and oral testimony in support and
opposition to the revised proposed bill.
Your Committee received a computer-generated presentation from the Department of
Planning, entitled "Proposed Moratorium on New Transient Vacation
Accommodations," and discussed revisions as recommended to the Commission,
which your Committee supported.
Your Committee further amended the revised proposed bill to delete language
extending the moratorium in an additional six months; delete Title 20 which was
confirmed to be redundant by Department of Corp. Counsel; and add an exception to
allow modifications to permits that existed prior to the effective date of the moratorium
but do not increase the number of units.
Your Committee voted 5-2 to recommend passage ofthe proposed bill on first reading
and filing ofthe communications.
And at this time, I'd like to propose an amendment based on the testimony that we
heard today.

many reasons. One is the Maui Island Plan prescribes and basically mandates us to
try to keep this ratio to . . . we're already over the ratio as described in the Maui Island
Plan.
But also, we make a commitment by resolution to a circular economy. And tourism is
one of the most extractive parts of our economy that we could have. They have multi-
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 152
national corporations coming in and setting up camp and taking millions of dollars out
of our economy with the, you know, with, that go to their headquarters on a daily basis.
We have a phenomenal amount of, of greenhouse gas emissions in the aviation that
brings the tourists here. But we are going to have address those aviation issues. I
think they're purport, purported to be about 50 percent of the emissions for the total
State. And we're not even counting them right now. We're not counting any of the
flights that go in and out internationally. And we're only counting one way of the flights
that come domestically. So, we are going to have to address this one way or another.
We need to start now, as soon as possible. This is a really important move.
And this is also, the other part of this is it's going to keep us from digging ourselves in
deeper in this hole that we're in. That the over-dependence on tourism is what got us
into this big trouble when the pandemic hit. Everything came to a halt. And because
tourism was basically our, our largest and almost only part of our economy, we had the
highest unemployment rate in the country. We can't allow ourselves to keep doing
that, and we have to put a stop to that. And that's what this moratorium is about.
You know, we got to force our, if we have to force ourselves to look for other ways to
expand our economy and make it about the local people, how more local people can
participate and contribute and benefit from the economy, instead of just having $12
and $15 an hour jobs where they have to work two or three or sometimes four jobs just
to make a living. We need those family wage earning jobs. We need careers. And
you know, we talk a lot about, about people, the young people leaving our, our islands
because they can't find houses. Well, the, and just as big a reason is they can't find
career pathways. And they don't want to work for $15 an hour for the rest of their life
and have to work two or three jobs.
We have got to start building our economy in a circular way that gives those career
options. And, and we can't afford to continue to dig ourselves deeper into this tourism
economy. We're not going to get there if we do that. We're just going to build more
dependence on it. And that's, it's almost an analogy for what we've been doing with
petroleum. We've been saying we want to cut our greenhouse gas emissions, but how
many people are still driving to, driving on petroleum on their way to the environmental
meetings? You know, you got to, at some point you got to just stop it, and you gotta
start, start doing, turning things around and, and do the right things.
So, so I, this is a good ... I thought the, the original moratorium was good. But I
understand why, why there was a move to, to keep us out of court. So, if it had to be
this.

Member Molina.
COUNCILMEMBER MOLINA: Thank you, Madam Chair. You know. I've never been a fan
of moratoriums, and it's certainly not an easy topic to deal with. But I've, you know,
changed my position on this. Because, you know, every so many years, every
community needs to do a self-assessment, you know, reassess things like growth and
its impact on the environment and the residents' quality of life.
So, I think, in my opinion, we are at a point in Maui County's history where we've seen
unprecedented popular, popularity of Maui County as a tourist destination, and
certainly other types of commerce has certainly grown, which is good. But however,
to use an old economics class term "opportunity costs", where every good opportunity,
there are costs that go with it. And the cost for Maui becoming so popular in terms of
tourism, it adds impacts such as more traffic congestion, more of our beach parks being
taken up so our residents, you know, don't have an opportunity to go there.
And then, of course, our keiki o ka aina and our kamaaina not having opportunities to
afford a house, that is really, really shameful. And I'm not sure if tourism is the primary
cause of that, but it is what it is. You know, as land costs go up and the rich folks come
here willing to pay anything, and of course part of it too is, you know, some of our
residents decide to sell. And sometimes you cannot blame them when someone's
going to offer millions of dollars for a piece of land. But all of that, you know, contributes
to what we're experiencing right now. All of these issues that have converged.
And maybe it is a time where we need to take that pause and reassess how we look
at tourism, how we want to manage it, and looking down the road towards economic
diversification and not be totally dependent on the tourism industry. So, I look at this
moratorium more as an opportunity to reset and make us really stop, pause, think about
what we really want to do. So, which is why, you know, I, I'm going to support this.
And, you know, the last moratorium we had was 30 years ago. So, maybe, you know,
and Maui County came, came out okay after that. And back in my first go around on
the Council, I remember a former colleague of mine Jo Anne Johnson, she always
used to talk about or try to champion carrying-capacity studies. So, we need to look
at how, how much population our County can handle. And so, that's why we need to
look at things like that.

BILL NO. 133 (20211
A BILL FOR AN ORDINANCE AMENDING CHAPTERS 19.64 AND 19.65, MAUI
COUNTY CODE, REU\TING TO BED AND BREAKFAST HOME PERMITS AND
SHORT-TERM RENTAL HOME PERMITS
CHAIR LEE: Member Paltin.
COUNCILMEMBER PALTIN:
I MOVE TO PASS BILL 133 (2021) ON SECOND AND FINAL
READING.
COUNCILMEMBER KING:
SECOND.
CHAIR LEE: Moved by Member Paltin, seconded by Member King, to pass Bill 133 on second
and final reading.
Discussion, Member Paltin?
COUNCILMEMBER PALTIN: The purposes of the proposed bill are to allow Accessory
Dwelling Units (ADUs) to be eligible for Bed and Breakfast Homes and Short-Term
Rental Home permits; limit STRH permits to one person and one per property; improve
notification requirements for B&B and STRH permit applications; establish that family
trusts may hold B&B and STRH permits; and amend Chapters 19.64 and 19.65, Maui
County Code, for clarity and consistency.
Your Committee determined ADUs should continue to be limited to residential use to
help maximize the availability of long, of housing units for County residents. Your
Committee revised the proposed bill to delete the title's referenced to Chapter 19.35,
Maui County Code; delete SECTION 1, which would have amended Chapter 19.35;
and made other corresponding revisions.
Your Committee also revised the proposed bill to establish that STRH permits be, only
be issued for single-family dwellings constructed at least 15 years prior to the
application and owned by the applicant for at least 15 years. In addition, your
Committee revised the proposed bill to establish that B&B permits may only be issued
for single-family dwellings constructed at least five years prior to the application and
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 169
owned by the applicant for at least five years. Your Committee determined these
requirements should be countywide, rather than island-specific standards.
Your Committee concluded that the revised proposed bill would achieve an appropriate
balance by providing some additional benefits to B&B and STRH applicants and
permittees while also prioritizing the use of dwellings for long-term residential use.
Your Committee also noted the need to further revise the proposed bill to incorporate
recent and anticipated ordinances amending Chapters 19.64 and 19.65. I respectfully
ask for the Members support of my motion. Thank you.
CHAIR LEE: Further discussion? If not, all those in favor of the motion, please raise your
hand and say "aye".

BILL NO. 135 (2021)
A BILL FOR AN ORDINANCE ESTABLISHING CHAPTER 20.42,
MAUI COUNTY CODE. TO PROHIBIT THE SALE. USE, OR DISTRIBUTION OF
NON-MINERAL SUNSCREENS
CHAIR LEE: Councilmember King. Go ahead.
COUNCILMEMBER KING: ... Thank you. Chair.
I MOVE TO PASS BILL 135 (2021) ON SECOND AND FINAL
READING.
Regular Meeting of the Council of the County of Maui
November 19, 2021
Page 172
COUNCILMEMBER SINENCI;
SECOND.
CHAIR LEE; Moved by Member King, second by Member Sinenci, to pass Bill 135 on second
and final reading.
Member King, discussion?
COUNCILMEMBER KING: Thank you. Chair. Your Committee on Climate Action,
Resilience, and Environment met on July 21,2021, August 4, 2021, and October 20,
2021, to discuss the proposed bill. The Committee voted unanimously to recommend
passage on first reading. On November 5, 2021, the Council held first reading and
passed the proposed bill with a unanimous vote, with the exception of myself, who was
excused to attend COP26.
Bill 120, Bill 135 (2021) prohibits the sale, use, or distribution of non-mineral
sunscreens including those containing oxybenzone, octinoxate, or both. This bill does
not prohibit the sale, use, or distribution of a non-mineral sunscreen if a person has a
prescription issued by a licensed healthcare provider.
The bill will authorize the use, sale, and distribution of sunscreens containing the active
ingredient minerals, zinc oxide and titanium dioxide only. Both, both components
designated by FDA as "generally recognized as safe and effective", which is the
acronym GRASE; G-R-A-S-E.
Bill 135 has received overwhelming support from the County Administration; the
Department of Environmental Management; State agencies including Jeff Bagshaw
and Peter Landon of the Department of Land and Natural Resources who have worked
tirelessly to educate the public about the dangers of sunscreen chemicals to the coral
reefs in the 'Ahihi-Kina'u Natural Area Reserve; the leading world expert on this issue,
Dr. Craig Downs of the Haereticus Environmental Laboratory; Maui Nui Marine
Resource Council; Hawaii Wildlife Fund; Sierra Club; Pacific Whale Foundation;
Realtors Association of Maui; Kihei Community Association; Kahaluu Bay Education
Center, a program of the Kohala Center on the Big Island; Friends of Hanaumu Bay;
and approximately 70 Kihei Charter School students who each provided written
testimony.
Finally, I would like to thank the Climate Action, Resilience, and Environment Vice-
Chair Sinenci for introducing this bill in my absence last week, and my fellow
Councilmembers for their support. I ask for the Members continued support for this
bill. Mahalo.

November 19, 2021

19.04.040, Definitions, to define the area enclosed by the three-dimensional exterior
surfaces of the building or structure, including any open air areas between the posts
or the posts and the walls that hold up a roof, such as carports and covered decks.
This term was added to 19.12.020(G)(3) in Ordinance 5126 but not to 19.04.040, and
is necessary to define the spatial limitation of transient vacation rental use in a dwelling
unit.
Your Committee agreed to amend the bill to read, "or payment of general excise tax
and transient accommodation tax" at the end of Subsection 19.19.020(G)(2). And this
was just a test, the original bill that I had sent to the Planning Commission required
registration numbers. And so, then it came back as TMK numbers. And I did hear

County Clerk
Keisa Liu - Testimony for Agenda Item E
From:
Sent:
To:
'Jr i-ICE OF TriE
COUNTY CLERKSubject:
IYou don't often get email from keisaliu@gmail.com. Learn whv this is important
My name is Keisa Liu and I am testifying on agenda item E: Ceremonial Resolution Recognizing Hunger and
Homelessness Awareness Week November 13-21, 2021.
I support this resolution with reservations because being aware that there is hunger and homelessness in Maui County is
not enough. We need to do more if we want to reverse this on our islands. This week, I urge the Council and everyone in
Maui County, to let go of the phrase “refuser of services", as it does not paint a complete or accurate picture of our
chronically houseless of Maui County. To justify this, I would like to share my experience walking someone down the path
out of houselessness on Maui.
At the beginning of 2021, I was tasked with learning more about the houseless in Maui County for my new job with
Councilmember Johnson. I got in contact with Lisa Darcy from Share Your Mana and she informed me there were
community meetings every Friday at the Amala encampment. I attended several of them and listened to Amala residents
talk about problems in the community, come up with solutions, and plans to implement them. This was the first time I met
Warmth (I am changing their name to a characteristic about them to protect their privacy). Warmth came with notes. They
were articulate. They were friendly and kind. I was confused as to why they were homeless. Warmth was always at the
meetings.
Warmth is chronically homeless. They are a Vet. They are older and have several health issues. They are a "refuser of
services” or "jackass" according to our Mayor.
Warmth was one of the few that did not leave Amala until the very end. I helped them move their things. I saw in their face
defeat and desperation during this time. I saw shame and hopelessness cloud their judgement. But I didn't pry. I just
moved their things.
After the "clean up", I learned they were staying near the Uhaul Storage in Kahului. I heard rumors that the area was
going to be “cleaned up” as well but I didn’t know when. 1 happened to be driving by right when the State Department of
Transportation was finishing. Further down the road, I saw Warmth. I pulled into the Uhaul parking lot and walked up to
them. Again. I saw defeat and desperation. I saw shame and hopelessness in their eyes. I saw how physically and
mentally exhausted they were. I asked for them to come sit with me in the shade. I offered my water. I called for Maya.
While we waited, the Uhaul manager came out to tell us we couldn’t stay here. They didn’t want to call the police, but they
alluded to the fact that It was within their abilities. I asked what Warmth wanted to do. Warmth was tired and thirsty and
hungry. The road made it hard to hear, let alone think. Their solution was to find a quiet spot somewhere to rest. I offered
my home.
Maya showed up and helped me load Warmth’s things into my truck. She took them to get something to eat and get a
Covid test. Then she brought them to my house. Warmth ate dinner with my family that night. They showered and slept
into the afternoon. When they woke, 1 listened to Warmth tell me why it’s been so hard for them to take help. I listened to
what they missed about not being homeless. I listened to them express themselves freely without shame or guilt imposed
by their environment. After a couple days, I heard them say they were ready to stop living this way.  Ioffered to walk the
path out of houselessness with them.
The steps to permanent housing seems simple enough:
1. Get a Homeless Verification Letter (HVL)
2, Schedule an intake with a shelter
3. Use their programs and services to obtain permanent housing
But it’s actually a lot harder.
1
For Warmth to obtain a HVL, an outreach worker from Family Life Center needs to see where they slept the night before.
Warmth would only qualify for a HVL if the last place he slept was not in a house. Because 1 brought Warmth into my
house, they no longer qualified as homeless, even though they most certainly were. So we had to come up with a plan.
We made an appointment with Family Life Center at 10am. Then, we scouted around Kahului to find what looked like a
safe space for them to sleep for the night outside. We contacted Family Life Center and described where he would be and
took a picture of the area so they could find Warmth. We went back to my house, I contacted a group of women I work
with and asked if any of them could show up around Sam to get them breakfast and take them to a bathroom so they
could clean up and be ready for their intake at 10am. Warmth ate dinner with my family and then at 8pm,  idropped them
off with their sleeping bag and a small bag of supplies.
The next morning. I received a text from the outreach worker for Warmth. I told the worker that he is at the spot and
expecting to do the intake at 10am. Maya arrived around Sam and the outreach worker from Family Life Center was
already there, two hours early. She left to get breakfast for Warmth and when she returned, both the outreach worker and
Warmth were gone. I advised her to go to Family Life Center and I would be down later.
When I arrived, Warmth saw me and ushered me over. The outreach worker was not happy with my presence and when I
greeted them they were incredibly curt. At one point, the outreach worker turned to Warmth and asked if they could talk
privately so the outreach worker could get “some honest answers”. Even after Warmth said they were fine with us being
there, the outreach worker insisted. I asked if it was ok for me to wait outside and I told Warmth I would be there when
they left.
After some time, they returned with a copy of his ID and some good news. Warmth was told their letter was in the system
and they could now attempt to seek shelter. The HVL is valid for one month, so we have one month to seek shelter.
For the majority of our houseless community, in order to receive services from the County, they need to be in one of the
sponsored organization's facilities. In order to get into the facilities, they need to schedule an intake. In order to schedule
an intake, they have to call each organization every morning and ask if there is space. Most times, the intake line is busy
and they end up having to leave a message. There is no guarantee that a person from intake will call back after a
message is left.
If they are lucky enough to be able to schedule an intake, they need a valid homeless verification form, ID, and they must
want permanent housing at the time of intake.
At the time I picked up Warmth, their phone and wallet with their ID had been stolen by another houseless individual. If I
had not picked them up, they would not have been able to do an intake at all. They would have to rely on an outreach
worker finding them on their rounds and setting up an intake.
I need to point out that many of the houseless residents I have spoken with have told me they do not see outreach
workers out enough. When they do see them, many of the houseless residents feel the interactions are cold,
transactional, and, therefore, difficult for them to invest their trust in the outreach worker. Outreach workers have not
created a space of enough emotional safety for our houseless to believe they really want to help. This was one reason
why Warmth had decided not to take services from outreach workers.
But because Warmth was with me, we used my phone to call the Intake line at a facility they were comfortable with. We
called for several days. We would leave messages. Sometimes, we got a call back to tell us the facility was full. Honestly,
it was discouraging and stressful. We stayed persistent and hopeful. When they felt down about receiving so much of their
time, I would tell them they owe me a dinner in their new place. When they were embarrassed about how much I was
helping, I would tell them stories about how others helped me. When they expected more from themselves, I reminded
them of how much they had done, in spite of how difficult this journey had been. When they felt they were undeserving, I
showed them they were.
On Wednesday, I took Warmth down for their intake at a facility. The day had been a bit stressful and  Icould tell they
were reaching their limit. I went with them to keep them focused on the work and their goals. After their intake, I took
Warmth home to decompress and have one last dinner with us as a family. I helped them move in. Even as we were
moving into the space, we both recognized that the level of compassion and respect were not at the level needed. But I
reminded them I was still going to be with them.
I am still walking the path out of houselessness with them. Just because they are in a facility doesn't mean they are out of
houselessness. Warmth wants to use the programs and services that are at the facility. They are determined to get out of
houselessness. I am determined to walk beside them.
2
I wanted to share this story with you because this person has been labeled a “refuser of services”. That phrase allows us
all to see them as stubborn, unintelligent, undeserving, and worthless. It allows us to stop trying to connect with them. It
allows us to break the trust needed to walk the path out of houselessness.
So, today, as we reflect on this “awareness” that hunger and homelessness exists, I need us all to recognize that not only
does it exist but it continues because of the way we speak about our houseless, how we treat our houseless, and how we
create systems that make the path out of houselessness more punishing than houselessness itself.
Mahalo for your time and consideration.
Keisa Liu
3
Aloha Maui County Council;
Mahalo for having the courage to address this important issue:
Bill 135 will remove environmentally hazardous materials that have been plaguing our islands for
decades. It does what we have been unable to do with thousands of hours of outreach and education.
It recognizes that relying on consumers to make the right choices has not been enough, especially in the
face of advertising campaigns and green-washing marketing.
Bill 135 solves the problem at the store - where the problem begins. Education /outreach at the
water's edge, where people may not have other alternatives or have to change plans, is difficult
and reaches only a minority.
Bill 135 makes it clear that we are not fooled: we know that just re-formulating and renaming
each newly fabricated compound from a laboratory does not suddenly make a product safe for
the environment.
Bill 135 not only protects our reefs and all species that rely on them, it protects our food
resources from contamination by products that are not necessary for sun protection in people.
This is an issue of environmental justice: one consumer's choice of using toxic sun-protection
products does not override the rights of others to sea foods without benzenes in them.
1.
2.
3.
Some dermatologists who make and sell their own sun-care products may defend chemical sunscreens,
yet dermatologists who do not make / market products say that mineral sun-bocks work to prevent both
UVA and UVB rays from reaching your lower dermal layer, where skin-cancer damage begins. Quotes
from doctors were used by the tobacco industry to promote benefits from smoking, such as "calming
nerves and lowering blood pressure." More recently, many well-meaning doctors promoted opioids.
Between 2009 and 2018 the National Cancer Institute has recorded a rise in skin cancer rates of 1.4%
annually, despite the availability of chemical sunscreens. Chemical sunscreen compounds began being
sold in 1978 - if they are so effective why are skin cancer rates rising consistently?
Industry will claim that its important to maintain "choices" for consumers because some people, for
reasons of vanity, because of the way the "pasty white stuff' looks, won't use mineral sun-blocks and
that some react badly. Zinc oxide is used to treat diaper rash - the number of people who have
sensitivity to mineral products must be exceedingly rare. There's a possibility of risk in every option
(except clothing). Can industry representatives tell us the percentage of people who have reactions to
chemical sunscreens versus the number of people who have reactions to mineral sun-blocks? Can the
industry share statistics of the number of people who suffer from second-hand exposure to benzines
(who do not have a choice) versus those who get skin cancer? Can we compare economic loss of sales
to economic costs of contaminated food, damaged reefs, loss of tourism and loss of shoreline
protection?
These compounds are based on what chemists call benzines, rings of carbon. They are designed to
absorb a photon of sunlight, then break-down, releasing heat, raising the temperature of your skin - this
is one reason why it affects corals and causes them to bleach. This is the definition of a chemical
reaction, and its happening within your skin cells. The remnants of the chemical structures, benzine
rings, are now free to travel in your body, into your bloodstream, through your endocrine system and
eventually into your urine. Mineral sun-blocks are not absorbed by your skin, they sit on the surface,
reflecting sunlight.
We have al\A/ays had choices that have worked for generations: cover-up, seek shade between 10 and 2,
use sun-blocks. All the major manufactures already make and sell mineral-based sun-protection, they
just aren't as profitable because they can't be patented.
I have talked with several thousand people, one-on-one for the last seven years on the front-lines at
beaches and parking lots. Almost all have expressed one thing: "I just wish these things were illegal to
sell / were off the market."
We ban the sale or use of many destructive products in our society. If oversight for one industry is
missing on higher levels, its prudent for local governments to protect their resources and people.
Thank you for doing this important work.
Jeff Bagshaw
Information and Outreach Specialist

Testimony to Maui County Board
In Opposition to Bill 135
By Curtis A. Cole, PhD, Photobiologist and Sunscreen Expert
I wish to present some information and context to that information to the Maui County Board that
opposes the proposed ban to sales and use of sunscreens containing organic filters in Hawaii. I am by
training a physicist, photobiologist, and sunscreen expert whose career has spanned 12 years of
academic research in skin cancer induction, and 27 years in industry developing sunscreen testing
methodologies and sunscreen products. I am among the top global authors regarding sunscreens (CV
attached).
I believe Bill 135 will do a great disservice to the millions of residents and visitors to the beautiful Maui,
and will further promote the degradation of sunscreen products not only in Hawaii, but also for the
other 49 States in America. State and Local bans of certain sunscreen ingredients force sunscreen
manufacturers to reformulate products for all States and regions as it is not economically viable to
manufacture state specific product formulations according to these local regulations. Compliance with
both State and local regulations on top of the FDA mandated limitations on UV filters and
concentrations has led to the point where it is becoming nearly Impossible to meet the UV protection
needs of the consumer (particularly those at greatest risk of skin cancer and malignant melanoma) and
all the regulatory requirements with the best performing high UV protection broad spectrum products.
Why is it important to have ability to utilize organic UV filters In sunscreens?
Titanium dioxide and Zinc Oxide are inorganic particulate filters; however they behave the same way in
protecting skin as do the "chemical" organic filters. The biggest difference is that the organic filters have
far higher absorbance efficiency (measured by extinction coefficient) than do the particulate inorganic
filters, and they are Invisible on the skin compared to inorganic sunscreens. Below are pictures showing
visible and UV illumination of products with either 3% avobenzone or 20% nanosize ZnO, illustrating the
pasty white appearance of the ZnO product, versus the clear invisible Avobenzone. When illuminated
with UVA light, it is easy to see that the avobenzone is providing far superior blockage of the UV light
compared with the lighter appearing ZnO treated area.
In order to achieve minimal amounts of UVA protection from ZnO or Titanium dioxide, very high
concentrations of these filters to achieve the same protection provided by just a few % of the organic
UVA filter. At such high concentrations, even the nano particle size Titanium Dioxide and ZnO give a
pasty white appearance. As a result, consumers apply less of the sunscreen than is intended for use,
and then continue to rub the sunscreens on the skin in attempt to make them less visible, effectively
removing them in the process - resulting in even less protection.
Addition of iron oxide particulates to try to blend them to skin tones helps alleviate some of appearance
issues but results in staining of clothes and car seats and anything that touches the skin - further
alienating consumer use.

protection in a sunscreen.

If the organic filters are banned from use in the US by state and local regulations such as the proposed
bill, consumers will have no choice but that of poorer, less protective and unattractive sunscreen
products, and many will likely opt for no sunscreen at all or choose to vacation at a location where they
are permitted to use their preferred organic based sunscreen products.
Meanwhile, the science alleging that organic sunscreens are responsible for damage to coral reefs is not
proven, and in fact the latest critique of these allegations demonstrate that sunscreens are not present
in sea water in sufficient concentrations anywhere to cause damage to coraF (which others will submit
for your consideration).
I ask that you oppose Bill 135 and await further research needed to establish the true causes of coral
reef damage before restricting the use of organic sunscreen that are essential to good UV protection on
the beaches of Maui.
Sincerely,
Curtis Cole, PhD Photobiologist
