COUNTY CLERKYou don't often get email from angie@coconutcondos.com. Learn whv this is important
November 18th, 2021
Dear Maui County Councilmembers,
On behalf of Maui PRMA, the Vacation Rental Trade Council under the Maui Chamber of Commerce, we would like to
express our opposition to the proposed moratorium on transient accommodations, establishing chapters 19.98 and
20.41.
PRMA, the Professional Rental Management Association, is a coalition of professional property management
companies representing over 1600 legally zoned condominium vacation rental units throughout Maui. Our members
are licensed in the State of Hawaii, engaged in the management of legal vacation rental properties, primarily
condominiums, and comply with real estate license law and code of ethics. Our companies represent 216 years in
business, employing nearly 200 employees and over 300 independent contractors and vendors.
The visitor industry supports so many other small businesses, contributing to our island's overall economic health.
When this industry is stifled and suffers, jobs are lost and that creates broad waves of negative impacts that ripple
throughout our community. When our industry was shut down during COVID, West Maui in particular saw
unemployment rates in the 50%+ range with food lines that were 2-3 hours long. Changes to our industry affect our
local community, especially in the areas where many of the tourism related employees live and reside (West/South
Maui).
Ultimately, this will not stop visitors from traveling to Maui. We believe it will have other negative impacts such as
reduced industry revenue and increased costs for guests, which could potentially lead to stays in illegal units at a time
when we are trying to support legal vacation rentals and shut down Illegal operations. The council and planning office
have spent so much time and effort in establishing rules, permitting and oversight for legal vacation rentals. We would
support continued efforts in allowing permitted transient accommodations to apply and operate in order to continue
to collect GET & TAT revenue and reduce the number of illegal operators. If there is no means for homeowners
interested in pursuing legal permits, we run the risk of creating more illegal operators. At the end of the two years,
this moratorium may create a bigger problem than when it started.
On behalf of PRMA, we ask that you please focus your attention on the Maui Nui Destination Management Action Plan
(DMAP) for the islands of Maui, Molokai, and Lanai. We would prefer to see the Council focus on efforts to support
the DMAP and further this work instead of looking for ways to punish property owners and potentially inadvertently
create more illegal vacation rentals.
1
We welcome any discussion with you or a member of your administration and can be contacted at
Mauiprma(q>gmail.com.
Mahalo,
John Kevan
Angela Leone
Co-Founders of Maui Professional Rental Management Association (Maui PRMA)

COUNTY CLERKTestimony of Carmen Hulu Lindsey

Maui County Council
Regular Meeting
Bill 118
RELATING TO REAL PROPERTY DEDICATED AS 'AINA KOPUNA
November 19. 2021 Online Meeting9:00 a.m.
The Office of Hawaiian Affairs (OHA) SUPPORTS the passage of Bill 118, which would
protect Native Hawaiian and other Maui county families from unprecedented tax burdens that
may lead to the dispossession of their 'aina kupuna, or ancestral lands. Allowing such families
to pay the minimum tax on lands that have sustained them for at least three generations will
help to ensure that the speculation-driven increase in Maui property values does not further
dispossess Native Hawaiians from their ancestral lands, and erode away the living vestiges of
Maui's cultural heritage.
As a preliminary matter, OHA emphasizes that the Westernization of land tenure in
Hawai'i, and the distribution of land and power that occurred subsequent to the illegal
overthrow of the Hawaiian Kingdom, have had profound and unique impacts on the Native
Hawaiian people. Such impacts include the physical, spiritual, and socioeconomic harms arising
from the dispossession of Native Hawaiians' ancestral lands, and from the myriad other
circumstances that have strained or eroded away Native Hawaiians' connection to the 'aina
that have sustained their 'ohana since time immemorial.^ Sadly, much of the lands that Native
Hawaiians were able to acquire during Hawai'i's transition to a Western property system have
already been lost, abandoned, sold, seized, or stolen, and various factors today continue to
place great strain on the ability of Native Hawaiian families to continue maintaining a
connection to, and ownership of, their ancestral 'aina.
In addition, OHA notes that Native Hawaiian and kama'aina families who have been able
to maintain their ancestral lands for generations represent a foundation of Maui's historical and
cultural legacy. Their continued stewardship and presence on their lands helps to maintain
important connections to the past and to the 'aina that can uphold the unique values and
character of Maui's communities - a role that may be increasingly critical in light of a rapidly
transforming socioeconomic landscape resulting, in part, from the real estate trends the county
has been experiencing.
^ See, e.g., The Apology Resolution, Pub. L No. 103-150 ("(T]he health and well-being of the Native Hawaiian
people is intrinsically tied to their deep feelings and attachment to the land").
Maui County Council
Bill 118 - Relating to Real Property Dedicated as 'Aina Kupuna
November 19, 2021
Page 2
OHA therefore strongly supports the passage of Bill 118 to provide tax relief to parcels
dedicated as 'aina kupuna. This proposed bill will provide critical and much-needed protection
for the few remaining Native Hawaiian and other kama'aina families who have maintained their
family lands for at least three generations. Over the past several decades, the Western
commodification of land has manifested in a continuous and almost exponential surge in
property taxes, particularly in areas where real estate speculation and resort or luxury
development have driven property values to an all-time high.^ While taxes on lands that have
become subject to the lucrative activities of real estate speculators and developers may be an
appropriate and needed mechanism can provide the county with much-needed revenues, the
increase in property values and associated property taxes has also had devastating impacts to a
small number of Native Hawaiian and long-time Maui families; such families, facing an
unprecedented tax burden, have been forced to sell portions of their ancestral lands, or
monetize them in inappropriate and sometimes painful ways. The proposed bill would provide
these families with narrow and targeted relief, by allowing them to pay the minimum tax on
their ancestral lands in exchange for a commitment to not convey the lands to non-lineal
descendants, and to not use these lands for commercial purposes, with limited exceptions.
This measure would therefore allow MauKs families to maintain their ownership of and deep
connection to their ancestral lands, in the face of economic and real property trends well
beyond their control - preventing the further dispossession of Native Hawaiians^ ancestral
lands, and protecting a historical foundation of Maui's unique values and cultural character
from further erosion.
OHA does appreciate potential concerns regarding  apossible reduction in county
revenues, but notes that the narrow requirements of the proposed bill would significantly limit
those who would qualify, and who would desire to apply for, the proposed tax relief. OHA
further understands that property values of non-'aina kupuna lands have also continued and
will likely further continue to increase,^ providing increased county revenues that will offset any
fiscal impacts of the measure.
Bill 118 will provide a critical means to protect Native Hawaiian and long-time kama'aina
families from the impacts of Maui's speculation-driven real estate boom; prevent the further
dispossession of Native Hawaiians' ancestral lands, which has had and will continue to have
devastating impacts on Native Hawaiian families and the Native Hawaiian community as a
whole; and help to avoid the further erosion of the social and cultural heritage of Maui county,
^ See Puanani Fernandez Akamine, Taxed Out: Increasing land taxes force Hawaiians off their ancestral lands, KA
WAl OLA, Feb. 28, 2020, available at https://kawaiola.news/cover/taxed-out.
^ See, e.g., Redfin.Com, Maui County Housing Market: Trends, https://www.redfin.com/countv/667/HI/Maui-
Countv/housing-market (last accessed Oct. 19, 2021) ("In September 2021, Maui County home prices were up
19.8% compared to last year, selling for a median price of $863K. On average, homes in Maui County sell after 69
days on the market compared to 86 days last year. There were 288 homes sold in September this year, up from
187 last year.").

embodied by those who have been able to maintain their 'aina kupuna for multiple
generations.
Accordingly, OHA respectfully urges the Maui County Council to PASS Bill 118 on second
reading. Mahalo nui for the opportunity to testify.

Dear Members of the Maui County Council:
On behalf of the Hawaii Dermatological Society and the nearly 16,500 U.S. members of
the American Academy of Dermatology Association (AADA), we write concerning Bill 135,
legislation that would prohibit the sale, use or distribution any non-mineral sunscreen
without a prescription. As dermatologists we dedicate ourselves to promoting habits in
our patients that ensure healthy skin. UV radiation damages the skin's DNA, which is the
first stage of skin cancer. We oppose this legislation and urge you to strongly consider the
broad implications of banning the use of sunscreens containing certain ingredients,
bearing in mind the grave dangers of sun exposure without adequate protection that the
residents and visitors of Maui County face.
Chemical (organic) sunscreen filters are an important component of many sunscreen
products. They provide ample broad-spectrum protection against UV radiation. This
legislation would remove access to chemical filters, leaving only mineral filters, which are
less effective filters. Mineral sunscreen products often leave a whitish residue on the skin.
Many, especially individuals with darker skin tones, find these to be unacceptable for use.

UV light exposure is a risk factor for all types of skin cancer and sunscreen use is a major
photo-protective method. UVA radiation damages deeper layers of the skin and
contributes to the development of melanoma, the deadliest form of skin cancer. UVB
radiation is the primary cause of sunburn and plays a key role in the development of skin
cancer in the skin's superficial layers. Both types of rays suppress the immune system.
Unprotected sun exposure is the most preventable risk factor for skin cancer. At least one
in five Americans will develop skin cancer,
cancer, is the second most common form of cancer in women, aged 15-29 years old.
Caucasian men, age 50+ are at a high risk of developing melanoma,
cases of melanoma are expected in Hawaii. ^ The annual cost of treating nonmelanoma
skin cancer in the U.S. is estimated at $4.8 billion, and the average annual cost of treating
melanoma is approximately $3.3 billion.

To prevent skin cancer, the AADA recommends comprehensive sun protection that
includes seeking shade; wearing protective clothing, including hats and sunglasses; and
applying a broad-spectrum, water-resistant sunscreen with an SPF of 30 or higher to
exposed skin.
Dermatologists have an interest in patient and public access to safe and effective
sunscreen ingredients. The FDA is currently working with industry on safety testing for
currently marketed sunscreen ingredients. The FDA is also considering several time-and-
extent applications (TEAs) for new sunscreen ingredients to be added to the FDA over-the-
counter (OTC) sunscreen monograph. The FDA's conclusion from recent studies on
sunscreen ingredient absorption "supports the need for further studies to determine the
clinical significance of these findings." FDA further stated that "these findings do not

indicate that individuals should refrain from the use of sunscreen." ^ Sunscreen
ingredients have been in use for almost 50 years without any reported systemic adverse
side effects. This issue highlights the urgent need for new safe and effective sunscreen
ingredients in the United States. With the approval of ingredients that utilize alternative
UV filters, the public's health will be better. The AADA continues to participate in the
discussion with the FDA and manufacturers regarding availability of current and new
ingredients.
We are concerned about the potential environmental impact of UV-filters. The potential
adverse effects related to the levels of UV-filters in the water supply and marine life (as
well as humans) are an emerging science. A review of 12 studies evaluating 14 different
organic UV filters in seawater near coral reefs determined that the majority of
concentrations found in seawater were in the nanograms per liter range. Nine papers
reported toxicological findings from no response to a variety of biological effects; these
effects were detected in the micrograms per liter to milligrams per liter range, namely, at
least 1000-fold higher than those reported in seawater in real life. The review concludes
"there is currently limited evidence to suggest that corals are adversely impacted by
environmental exposure to UV filters."
Our organizations advocated for the enactment of the Further Consolidated
Appropriations Act, 2020, by which the U.S. Congress directed the Environmental
Protection Agency (EPA) to contract with the National Academy of Sciences (NAS) to
conduct a scientific literature review of current sunscreens' potential risk to the marine
environment. The study will consider scientific literature on the potential public health
implications as a result of reduced use of sunscreens. This type of research is necessary to
understand how UV filters may affect the environment. We encourage you to consider
these ongoing efforts before taking any action to remove a product that has been proven
to be effective against skin cancer. Based on current data, removing specific sunscreen
ingredients and products from the market would be premature. Doing so would deprive
the public of an integral component of photoprotection to decrease the risk of skin
cancer.

Please consider the public health consequences of removing access, banning use, or
attaching stigma to sunscreens containing certain ingredients. We oppose Bill 135 for the
reasons above, and we request that Hawaii give the FDA more time to evaluate new
sunscreens for public use and for the NAS to conduct its review and report its findings. If
this bill gains support, we urge the Council to 1) remove the word "use," so the ban only
applies to sale and distribution and 2) extend the effective date to allow dermatologists
and other physicians and pharmacies adequate time to deveiop and implement a
prescribing infrastructure. We appreciate the opportunity to provide written comments
on this important public health issue. For more information, please contact Lisa Albany,
director of state policy for the AADA, at LAIbany@aad.org or (202) 712-2615.

November 17, 2021
Dear Maui County Council
I am writing to you today to express my support for Bill 135. This law will help everyone, especially
the coral reefs and make the land healthier. Banning harmful sunscreen ingredients will be
challenging but we can do it, and it will be worth it.
A lot of the time, sunscreen brands say that they are “reef friendly”, or “reef safe”, but if you take a
look, they still contain the harmful ingredients that have negative impacts on the coral reefs. Also, a
lot of people might think “corals are just rocks, why should we care about them?" They’re not just
rocks. Coral reefs are living things that serve as homes to many marine organisms, and they also
create our waves; if you surf, you should start being mindful of the sunscreen you are using.
Some ingredients included in sunscreen that are harmful to the reef, and even humans include
Oxybenzone, Octinoxate, Homosalate, Octisalate, Octocrylene, Avobenzone, and many others. All of
these chemicals are in sunscreen and commonly used today, which is incredibly disappointing.
Creating this law will change a lot. I understand that some people might not agree with this law, and
might still want to use whatever sunscreen they want, but if we keep on letting this happen, our reefs
might die in a couple of years, which we don’t want to happen. Please, do the right thing.

IYou don't often get email from misslily718@yahoo.com. Learn why this is important
Aloha dear council members.
I learned while living in Maui that the original Hawaiian people had in place systems to protect their water. At the very
top of the waterfalls or springs the area would be kapu or off limits. The next area down was strictly for drinking water
and cooking. Farther down they had an area for bathing their bodies, next dishes and other things that need be washed.
Now, here is the interesting part: before the water would run off to the sea the Hawaiian's in fact built a sort of filtration
system. They built this filter so that nothing would run out to the ocean that they didn't want. Food scraps, hair etc.
They did this hundreds of years ago because they knew that if anything went into the ocean it would affect the reefs.
They knew that if the reefs were affected the fish would go away. If the fish went away they knew that not only would
their people starve but the ocean would be imbalanced. The Hawaiian's understood that delicate balance and would not
pollute the very ocean they needed to survive and to thrive!
How is it today that we have any question at all about the legitimacy and dire necessity of this bill? Not only should any
toxic chemicals be banned but there should be extreme repercussions for those who break this ban like back in the days,
a kapu! Why would we condone the sales of toxins that we 100% know will end up in our oceans to harm our reefs is
beyond my comprehension. The Hawaiian's knew it and perhaps we can take a hint!
This is serious. This is our ecosystem and reefs at stake! We need this bill to be the first of many! We need these changes
now!
Mahalo for your time. I hope you choose wisely and honor the ways of the people who inhabited and were stewards of
these lands.

I am a local Maui resident and lover of the oceans and nature. It's been far too long that we as a people have let the
interests of corporations and profits take president over the health and wellness of our lands and its inhabitants. Let us
be the first county to pass legislation prohibiting toxic chemicals being sold and poured into our waterways.
The reefs are dying. Every time 1 go snorkeling 1 see the yellow discoloration of the reefs. This is not normal and cannot
be justified away. We have science and many documentaries proving that the reefs are dying at an alarmingly
accelerated rate. Should we not at least try in any way we can? I ask that we as a people put our beliefs into action. We
know this stuff is poisonous to the ecosystem. Why would we package it, sell it and worse, put it on our bodies? I
understand these companies want to turn a profit and they probably make a lot of money stocking up Hawaii's
sheives....but at what point will wellness, harmony and respect for nature be more important than a corporation's
bottom line???
These companies will oppose this bill and have incredibly compelling arguments I'm sure; crafter by skilled writers and
legal teams. But ask yourselves today: what is right? What is pono?

COUNTY CLERKTimeshare with
November 18, 2021
TO: Councilmember Alice Lee, Chair
Councilmember Keani Rawlins-Femandez, Vice- Chair
Members of the Maui County Council
FR: AMERICAN RESORT DEVELOPMENT ASSOCIATION - HAWAII (ARDA-Hawaii)
RE: Opposition to CR 21-130 ("A BILL FOR AN ORDINANCE ESTABLISHING
CHAPTER 19.98, MAUI COUNTY CODE. DECLARING A MORATORIUM ON
NEW TRANSIENT ACCOMMODATIONS ON MAUI")
Hearing date: Friday, November 19, 2021 at 9:00AM
Aloha Chair Lee, Vice Chair Rawlins-Fernandez and members of the Planning and Sustainable
Land Use Committee,
Thank you for allowing ARDA-Hawaii to submit testimony in STRONG OPPOSITION to CR
21-130 regarding a bill for an ordinance establishing Chapter 19.98, Maui County Code, declaring
a moratorium on new transient accommodations on Maui. CR 21-130 seeks to create an island
wide moratorium which temporarily prohibits the issuance of any permit under Title 19. The
proposed measure creates multiple concerns that could have broad impacts on Maui’s economy.
The stated purpose of the proposed bill is to establi.sh a moratorium to comply with visitor to
resident ratio specified in Chapter 2.SOB of the Maui Island Plan by pausing the increase of
transient accommodations. However, there is no data to suggest that stopping the development of
transient accommodations will decrease the number of visitors to Maui. It is simple, it won’t.
Visitors will still come to Maui and seek other accommodations even if the supply of lodging units
is limited. As we have seen, the volume of visitors will permeate into the residential neighborhoods
and long-term apartment inventory on Maui through an increase in short-term vacation rentals
("STR"), which are the fastest-growing segment within Maui's visitor accommodations industry.
If the purpose of the bill is to ultimately decrease the visitor count, perhaps further regulation is
needed on the number of illegal short-term vacation rental units that are allowed.
There are better alternatives to accomplish the Council’s goals of reducing the impacts of the
tourism industry on Maui residents, without the negative economic impacts created by the
proposed moratorium. Regulating on the fastest growing segment of transient accommodations in
1
Maui would provide the greatest reduction in the number of visitors to comply with the Maui Island
Plan.
Conversely, the moratorium would seek to limit transient accommodations island wide, thus,
prohibiting increased accommodations even in districts that are intended for tourism. Hotel and
resort areas are where visitor accommodations should be focused and aid in insulating Maui
residents from the perceived negative impacts of tourism.
In addition, there are several legal concerns associated with the procedure and public transparency
of CR 21-130. The legislation appears to be rushed with it being heard by the Planning and
Sustainable Land Use Committee before receiving and having the opportunity to consider the
Commission's minutes. Further, the proposed moratorium may be unconstitutional as it: 1) fails to
substantially advance a legitimate government interest as required by the due process clause; 2) is
unconstitutionally vague; 3) and it violates the equal protection clause in treating similarly situated
individuals differently.
Moreover, the moratorium will have substantial economic impacts on Maui's economy. It is
concerning that the County would seek to deter capital investment in a time where it is still
recovering from the pandemic. This is especially concerning given the recent surge in cases that
may continue to impact the tourism industry. The County would risk forgoing substantial amounts
of investments, tax revenues and job creation for Maui’s economy. For example, the timeshare
industry is currently a major contributor to Maui’s economy by providing more than $73 million
per year in state and local taxes, sustaining occupancies to keep Maui residents employed and
supplying well compensated jobs to Maui residents.
The proposed ordinance would enact a moratorium to “maintain the status quo” while the Tourism
Management Temporary Investigative Group ("TIG") explores and recommends legislation to
better manage the tourism industry’s impact on the environment and residents’ quality of life. This
poses significant risk to Maui’s economy, without fust obtaining information to better manage the
industry. Instead, the TIG, in collaboration with the tourism industry, could address the Council’s
concerns prior to the moratorium to prevent causing unnecessary harm to Maui’s fragile economy,
which is still recovering from the pandemic.
Additionally, the proposed ordinance is intended to mitigate negative impacts on the environment
and residents’ quality of life. Again, there is a lack of data suggesting that a moratorium will
substantially reduce the negative impacts on the environment or residents. It is concerning that
such a broad and inflexible policy would be implemented without first showing transient
accommodations as the primary cause of these negative impacts. As mentioned previously, further
regulation of STRs would reduce the negative impacts on resident's quality of life. Measures
eliminating illegal STRs would accomplish the moratorium’s intended goals without implementing
such an inflexible policy that would result in lost jobs and reduced economic activity for residents.
Further, as (a member of/chair) of ARDA-Hawaii, which represents the vacation ownership and
resort development industries in Hawaii, I’m smprised that the County would want to stop
timeshare visitors from coming to Maui. The timeshare visitor has the exact profile of the type of
visitor that we should welcome with higher income levels, more spending off property in
2
restaurants and local stores, and are more likely to take care of their property and Maui County.
Maui is a “home away from home” as Timeshare visitors they are owners, not transients. We
should be mindful that the timeshare industry could be part of the solution.
Due consideration should be given to the broader impacts of the proposed measure. We as a
community should consider a resolution that provides our Maui residents with the ability to
continue to work and provide for their families, while at the same time addressing the impacts of
the influx of visitors, until Maui can transition to a more diversified economic base.
I respectfully ask that you recommend the Council defer this measure and instead pursue the
further regulation of STRs prior to implementing any form of moratorium to address the Council's
concerns in a more meaningful way.
Thank you for your consideration of my comments.
Respectfully submitted
Ryan Nobriga
Maui Task Force Chair
ARDA Hawaii

Honorable Maui County Councilmembers
I am in strong support of Bill 135 prohibiting the sale, use. or distribution of non-mineral sunscreens that
have questionable effects on the health of humans and marine life. On September 24, 2021, the FDA
issued a proposed order concerning nonprescription sunscreen drug products. In the proposed order, two
mineral products zinc oxide and titanium dioxide are deemed generally recognized as safe and effective
(GRASE).
I constantly remind folks that the "reef friendly" labeling that companies put on their products really only
means that the chemicals they put in those products are legal. They are not safe or friendly.
In some other countries non-mineral chemicals such as avobenzone, octocrylene etc. are banned.
Our islands deserve the same protections.
Mahalo for your consideration
John E Palmer
Reef Education Volunteer at Kahalu'u Beach Park

COUNTY CLERK
I am writing to you today to express my support for Bill 135.1
reasons and opinions helpful in supporting Bill 135.
Certain chemicals in sunscreen, such as Octocrylene or Homosalate are toxic to
coral reefs, aquatic life, and even humans, due to the fact that most of the harmful
chemicals in sunscreen are able to penetrate the skin. There are many scientific
studies and reports that prove the harmful impacts that chemical-based sunscreen
ingredients have on living organisms. Here is a list of the chemicals found in
sunscreen that prove to be harmful toward humans and the environment:
Avobenzone. Benzophenone, Cylcopentasilozane/Cyclomethicone, Formaldehyde,
Homosalate. Methylisothiazolinone, Microbeads, Nanoparticles, Octocrylene,
Octinoxate, Parabens, Phthalates, Quaternium-15, Retinyl Palmitate, Sodium
Lauryl/Laureth Sulfate, and Zinc Oxide.
Coral reefs provide places for aquatic organisms to thrive, so without them, we
would have a shortage of fish as food. Coral reefs also provide oxygen for humans
and living organisms to breathe.
Please consider what I have shared today when you are deliberating about passing
Bill 135.
Sincerely,
Hope Patterson
7th Grade Student at Kihel Charter School

Re: Concerns Regarding the Moratorium on New Transient Accommodations on Maui
(CR 21-130)
Dear Chair Lee and Councilmembers:
My name is Karl Reul, and I am a General Manager at Kaanapali Golf Courses. I write on
behalf of Kaanapali golf Courses to recommend three changes to the bill that I believe will better
balance tourism management concerns with economic concerns.
Visitors have come to expect updated and modern accommodations, which means hotels and
resorts must be able to repair, maintain, redevelop and remodel accommodations. The high
quality of our accommodations is what provides a consistent and reliable source of revenue that
allows our Kaanapali golf Coursesto be an economic resource for the community. Limiting or
restricting the ability to redevelop accommodations will detrimentally impact our ability to
continue to be that resource.
Given these concerns, we respectfully request the Council revise its draft of the bill in
accordance with the following:
(1) Remove “lodging capacity’7“structural footprint” and replace with “increase existing number
of transient accommodation units” in Section 19.98.040. This addresses our ability to update
existing hotel amenities, restaurants and lobbies.
(2) Add clarifying language that the moratorium ends upon the passage of the first piece of
legislation that adopts any recommendation made by the temporary investigative group in
Section 19.98.050. The current terminology of “implementation” of “recommendation” is vague
and open to interpretations. The trigger to end the moratorium must be stated clearly.
(3) Define the term “operation” in Section 19.98.020 to mean “(1) The issuance of a certificate of
occupancy, operating license, or equivalent governmental approval for a hotel, timeshare, or
transient vacation rental; or (2) The issuance of  ashort-term rental home permit or equivalent
government approval for a short-term vacation rental.”
November 18, 2021
Page 2
I appreciate the opportunity to provide testimony on this bill and hope the Council will
carefully consider these issues before voting on the final version.
Very truly yours,
Karl J.Reul, GM
Kaanapali Golf Courses

Members of the Maui County Council

Dear Members of the Maui County Council,
I write to you on behalf of AIM at Melanoma, AIM's companion organization The Skin Cancer
Education & Research Foundation, and my family concerning Bill 135, legislation that would
prohibit the sale, use, and distribution of any non-mineral sunscreen without a prescription.
Please consider delaying this legislation until more definitive Information is available on the
potential environmental adverse effects.
My family has owned a home on Maui since I was a teenager, and before that we rented homes
on our many visits. My parents, children, husband, and I have and continue to spend a lot of
time on the island over the years. My husband is  aStage ill melanoma survivor. In fact, we
discovered his melanoma on Oneioa Beach in 2013 when he showed me a mole on his arm that
had changed from in color from brown to black. I am the vice president of AIM at Melanoma, a
global non-profit whose goal is to end this disease in our lifetime while improving the lives of
those it affects.
And melanoma affects a lot of people. In 2021, an estimated 207,390 cases of melanoma will be
diagnosed in the United States, about half of those invasive cases^ Over 7,000 people will die of
the disease^. In 2019, the estimate for the number of new invasive melanoma cases diagnosed
in the U.S. was 40% higher than the estimate in 2009^. But it's not just melanoma that's
concerning: Squamous and basal cell skin cancer diagnoses number over 5 million every year^,
and the estimates for squamous cell skin cancer deaths are estimated in the multiple thousands
each year. For many millions of people, skin cancers are disfiguring, costly, and frightening.
^ American Cancer Society. "Cancer Facts and Figures 2021". Atlanta; American Cancer Society; 2021.
2 American Cancer Society. "Cancer Facts and Figures 2021". Atlanta: American Cancer Society; 2021.
3 American Cancer Society. "Cancer Facts and Figures 2019". Atlanta: American Cancer Society; 2019.
American Cancer Society. "Cancer Facts and Figures 2020". Atlanta: American Cancer Society; 2020.
#endmelanoma
AIM at Melanoma is a 501(c)(3) organization. Tax ID #56-2427805
But skin cancers are mostly preventable because the vast majority are caused by UV damage
from the sun^. Sunscreen is a key component to protect skin from UV damage. AIM at
Melanoma stresses using broad spectrum sunscreen of 30 SPF or higher daily; wearing
protective clothing, hats, and sunglasses; and seeking shade. Additionally, we warn that it's
critical to avoid sunburns, especially blistering sunburns, as your risk for melanoma doubles if
you've had more than five sunburns^ One blistering sunburn in childhood or adolescence more
than doubles your chances of developing melanoma later in life^.
Banning the sale, use, and distribution without a prescription of the entire category of organic
(chemical) sunscreens is an enormous public health concern. This legislation removes access to
chemical filters, leaving only the less effective mineral filters. Additionally, it's essential to
remember that sunscreen is only effective if used—and used as directed. Many people prefer
the feel, texture, and look of chemical sunscreens, because mineral sunscreens can feel "heavy"
on the skin and leave a whitish residue. People won't use a sunscreen they don't like, or they
won't use the right amount of it, and that leaves them vulnerable to UV damage and all types of
skin cancer.
The FDA is currently working with industry on safety testing for currently marketed sunscreen
ingredients, and the FDA is also considering several time-and-extent applications (TEAs) for new
sunscreen ingredients to be added to the FDA over-the-counter (OTC) sunscreen monograph.
AIM at Melanoma applauds the consideration of these new ingredients: We hope the U.S. can
soon market filters that have been used safely in Europe for many years. And we understand the
FDA's further safety testing of currently approved sunscreen ingredients, while we highlight the
FDA's own language that "Absorption does NOT equal risk - The FDA advises continued use of
sunscreens. The findings in these studies do not mean that the FDA has concluded that any of
the ingredients tested are unsafe for use in sunscreens, nor does the FDA seeking further
information indicate such."
As for environmental concerns, AIM joined many other melanoma and skin cancer-related
organizations to advocate for the National Academy of Sciences (NAS) study authorized in late
2019 that looks at current data gaps related not only to sunscreen access and public health but
also to the effects of sunscreen on the environment. It was clear that more research is needed
to understand any potential environmental concerns. That report is expected in Spring 2022 and
will give us all guidance on sunscreens, human health, and the environment. We eagerly await
the results of this study.
On behalf of AIM at Melanoma, The Skin Cancer Education & Research Foundation, and my
family, I urge Maui County to delay this legislation until the NAS study is published—and
reconsider It at that time, with more information on any true environmental concerns as well
as a greater understanding of the public health threat that this ban would create. As Hawaii's
own DOH noted in a letter to The Honorable Kelly Takaya King of your council dated July 30,
5 Parkin DM, Mesher D, Sasieni P. Cancers attributable to solar (ultraviolet) radiation exposure in the
UK in 2010. Br J Cancer 2011; 105;S66-S69.
® Pfahlberg A, Kolmel KF, Gefeller 0. Timing of excessive ultraviolet radiation and melanoma:
epidemiology does not support the existence of a critical period of high susceptibility to solar
ultraviolet radiation-induced melanoma. Br J Dermatol 2001; 144:3:471-475.
^ Lew RA, Sober AJ, Cook N, et al. Sun exposure habits in patients with cutaneous melanoma: a case
study. J Dermatol Surg One 1983; 12:981-6.
#endmelanoma
AIM at Melanoma is a 501(c)(3) organization. Tax ID #56-2427805
2021: "From a skin cancer/human health perspective, it is not advisable to limit sunscreens to
only zinc/titanium as the active ingredients. Some people have hypersensitivity type reactions to
these ingredients, or just don't like them and would likely not use sunscreen at all if these were
the only options. In addition, research is definitely needed to better understand the risks to the
environment from other chemical sunscreen constituents." We completely agree.

Aloha Chair Lee, Vice-Chair Rawlins-Fernandez, and members of council, xi Oj=m j=
My name is Shelby Serra and I am testifying today on behalf of Pacific Whale
Foundation, whose mission is to protect the ocean through science and advocacy,
and to inspire environmental stewardship.
I am testifying in support of Bill 135.
We would like to reiterate our previous points, as well as provide feedback to the
opposition.
Ultimately, coral reefs across the globe are threatened by a number of human
impacts; agricultural runoff, over sedimentation, ocean acidification, overfishing,
tourism pressure. Although the science on reef damage due to sunscreen chemicals
is emerging, we urge the precautionary approach. Why risk added yet another
stressor to and already stressed and incredibly fragile ecosystem?
Coral reefs are among the most biologically diverse ecosystems in the world,
supporting nearly one million species of algae, invertebrates, and fish. In Hawaii,
coral reefs house more than 7,000 known species of marine plants and animals,
almost 20% of which are endemic to Hawaii, they provide protection to our
coastlines from storm surge and hurricanes, and have over $33 billion in economic
value. The science is clear that many of the elements in chemical sunscreens are
harmful to coral and other marine life. Research has shown that some chemicals
commonly found in sunscreen can damage coral reefs by disrupting coral
reproduction, inhibiting growth, deforming coral DNA, and increasing the rate of
zooxanthellae viruses and coral bleaching.
In Dr. Downs' presentation to your committee last month, it was revealed that
adjustments to single polymers in a chemical compound used in a sunscreen can
yield a new compound but still have similar detriments to the ecosystem. However,
the change in name of that compound can exempt it from prohibiting law. By this
logic, implementing a law that allows only mineral-based sunscreens, such as those
based in zinc oxide and titanium dioxide, strengthens the intent of the legislation,
and disallows manufactures from finding loopholes by adjusting their chemical
compounds slightly.
1
PACIFIC WHALE FOUNDATIONProtecting the ocean through science and advocacy and inspiring environmental stewardship
According to the 2020 Hawai'i Ocean Resources Management Plan as well as the
Hawai'i Tourism Authority, around 10 million people visit Hawai'i every year. Of
this, it is reported that 80% of visitors take part in marine activities.
The opposition points to limiting the options for sun protection by removing
sunscreens that are chemical-based. Environmental waste, in many instances, can
be attributed to convenience (also known as many options for the same thing]. As an
organization, we encourage covering up over lathering on; this eliminates the need
to any sunscreen lotions, and eliminates the single use plastic waste associated with
sunscreen. Using human safety, and risk of skin cancer as a point to oppose this bill
is weak. If this bill passes, there will still be many safe and effective options to
combat the risk of skin cancer.
In addition, another point of the opposition is that the mineral-based sunscreens
leave a white residue. This is simply not the case across the board; there are some
mineral-based sunscreens that leave no residue.
Passing this law will create leverage at the State level to amend the current
prohibitions on harmful chemicals. We commend Maui County for, once again, being
a leader in environmental legislation.
Finally, we believe that a large-scale education campaign in conjunction with this
legislation would yield the most efficacy. Our research has shown that policy
without education and outreach is not effective; people need to know and need to be
educated on the "why" of the problem.
We must take swift action and take a different approach to sunscreen prohibitions
to ensure that we do not continue to add reef damaging chemicals into our waters,
harming our reefs and marine life, day after day.
Mahalo for your time.
Shelby Serra
Conservation Advocate
Pacific Whale Foundation

Aloha Council Chair Lee and Councilmembers,
I am in strong support of Bill 135, to prohibit the sale, use, or distribution of non-mineral sunscreens. Non-mineral
sunscreens are harmful to corals, the marine environment, and humans. Additionally, the active ingredients in non
mineral sunscreens have not been designated as generally recognized as safe and effective (GRASE) by the FDA. Lastly,
Bill 135 permits the sale, use, or distribution of non-mineral sunscreens with a prescription from a physician. There are
many mineral sunscreen options available to the general public.
Maui County has the opportunity to make history today by passing Bill 135.1 urge the Council to once again implement
the General Plan objective of protecting the natural environment and show the world it cares about the health of the
coral reefs, marine environment, and people.
Mahalo,
Nicole Siegel

Aloha Chair Lee, Vice Chair Rawlins-Fernandez, and members of the Maui County Council,
My name is Wayne Chung Tanaka and I am testifying in my personal capacity to express my continued, STRONG
SUPPORT for the 'aina kupuna tax relief proposal in Bill 118. This measure will provide significant, critical relief
necessary to prevent the further permanent loss of family 'aina that Native Hawaiian and other longtime residents of
Maui County have lived on and cherished for generations, and ensure that these lands can continue to support their
future generations -- thereby preserving the people and the heart of what makes Maui such a unique and special place
to live.
I am grateful for your favorable consideration of this bill in its first reading, and respectfully ask that you help to finally
pass this measure on second reading, and long-needed relief for those who have been pushed ever closer to the brink of
losing their connections to their ancestral lands.
Mahalo nui loa.
Wayne Chung Tanaka

Dear Members of the Maui County Council:
On behalf of the Hawaii Dermatological Society and the nearly 16,500 U.S. members of
the American Academy of Dermatology Association (AADA), we write concerning Bill 135,
legislation that would prohibit the sale, use or distribution any non-mineral sunscreen
without a prescription. As dermatologists we dedicate ourselves to promoting habits in
our patients that ensure healthy skin. UV radiation damages the skin's DNA, which is the
first stage of skin cancer. We oppose this legislation and urge you to strongly consider the
broad implications of banning the use of sunscreens containing certain ingredients,
bearing in mind the grave dangers of sun exposure without adequate protection that the
residents and visitors of Maui County face.
Chemical (organic) sunscreen filters are an important component of many sunscreen
products. They provide ample broad-spectrum protection against UV radiation. This
legislation would remove access to chemical filters, leaving only mineral filters, which are
less effective filters. Mineral sunscreen products often leave a whitish residue on the skin.
Many, especially individuals with darker skin tones, find these to be unacceptable for use.

UV light exposure is a risk factor for all types of skin cancer and sunscreen use is a major
photo-protective method. UVA radiation damages deeper layers of the skin and
contributes to the development of melanoma, the deadliest form of skin cancer. DVB
radiation is the primary cause of sunburn and plays a key role in the development of skin
cancer in the skin's superficial layers. Both types of rays suppress the immune system.
Unprotected sun exposure is the most preventable risk factor for skin cancer. At least one
in five Americans will develop skin cancer,
cancer, is the second most common form of cancer in women, aged 15-29 years old.
Caucasian men, age 50+ are at a high risk of developing melanoma,
cases of melanoma are expected in Hawaii. ^ The annual cost of treating nonmelanoma
skin cancer in the U.S. is estimated at $4.8 billion, and the average annual cost of treating
melanoma is approximately $3.3 billion. ®

To prevent skin cancer, the AADA recommends comprehensive sun protection that
includes seeking shade; wearing protective clothing, including hats and sunglasses; and
applying a broad-spectrum, water-resistant sunscreen with an SPF of 30 or higher to
exposed skin.
Dermatologists have an interest in patient and public access to safe and effective
sunscreen ingredients. The FDA is currently working with industry on safety testing for
currently marketed sunscreen ingredients. The FDA is also considering several time-and-
extent applications (TEAs) for new sunscreen ingredients to be added to the FDA over-the-
counter (OTC) sunscreen monograph. The FDA's conclusion from recent studies on
sunscreen ingredient absorption "supports the need for further studies to determine the
clinical significance of these findings." FDA further stated that "these findings do not

indicate that individuals should refrain from the use of sunscreen,
ingredients have been in use for almost 50 years without any reported systemic adverse
side effects. This issue highlights the urgent need for new safe and effective sunscreen
ingredients in the United States. With the approval of ingredients that utilize alternative
UV filters, the public's health will be better. The AADA continues to participate in the
discussion with the FDA and manufacturers regarding availability of current and new
ingredients.
»9 Sunscreen
We are concerned about the potential environmental impact of UV-filters. The potential
adverse effects related to the levels of UV-filters in the water supply and marine life (as
well as humans) are an emerging science. A review of 12 studies evaluating 14 different
organic UV filters in seawater near coral reefs determined that the majority of
concentrations found in seawater were in the nanograms per liter range. Nine papers
reported toxicological findings from no response to a variety of biological effects; these
effects were detected in the micrograms per liter to milligrams per liter range, namely, at
least 1000-fold higher than those reported in seawater in real life.The review concludes
"there is currently limited evidence to suggest that corals are adversely impacted by
environmental exposure to UV filters."
Our organizations advocated for the enactment of the Further Consolidated
Appropriations Act, 2020, by which the U.S. Congress directed the Environmental
Protection Agency (EPA) to contract with the National Academy of Sciences (NAS) to
conduct a scientific literature review of current sunscreens' potential risk to the marine
environment. The study will consider scientific literature on the potential public health
implications as a result of reduced use of sunscreens. This type of research is necessary to
understand how UV filters may affect the environment We encourage you to consider
these ongoing efforts before taking any action to remove a product that has been proven
to be effective against skin cancer. Based on current data, removing specific sunscreen
ingredients and products from the market would be premature. Doing so would deprive
the public of an integral component of photoprotection to decrease the risk of skin
cancer.

Please consider the public health consequences of removing access, banning use, or
attaching stigma to sunscreens containing certain ingredients. We oppose Bill 135 for the
reasons above, and we request that Hawaii give the FDA more time to evaluate new
sunscreens for public use and for the NAS to conduct its review and report its findings. If
this bill gains support, we urge the Council to 1) remove the word "use," so the ban only
applies to sale and distribution and 2) extend the effective date to allow dermatologists
and other physicians and pharmacies adequate time to develop and implement a
prescribing infrastructure. We appreciate the opportunity to provide written comments
on this important public health issue. For more information, please contact Lisa Albany,
director of state policy for the AADA, at LAIbany@aad.org or (202) 712-2615.

Testimony for Bill 135
To:
Subject:
You don't often get email from wyattvanderlee@kiheicharter.org. Learn why this is important
)ear Maui County Council,
I am writing to express my gratitude and support for Bill 135 to ban the sale and use of non-mineral
sunscreen products that contain oxybenzone and octinoxate.
I fully support Bill 135 because non-mineral sunscreen is very harmful to reefs. We can see this as Hawaii's
reefs are slowly dying. This impact makes the reef pure white which may look beautiful but is actually very bad
because marine animals that take shelter and feed off the reef will lose their home and food supply. This will
affect the fishing community throughout Hawaii as they will catch fewer fish, negatively impacting local fish
supply. Visitors who come here to see the colorful reefs of Hawaii may be disappointed by the environmental
damage. This impact may negatively impact the tourist industry. But it is not only the marine animals that
mineral sunscreens affect. Sunscreen also causes water pollution. One example of this is if there are multiple
people in the ocean who are using non-mineral sunscreen. Swimmers using release chemicals in the water
that will sting your eyes when you dunk your head and get back up and open your eyes. This has happened to
me personally many times before. Furthermore, sunscreens are not F.D.A approved in the U.S., meaning they
are not actually as safe and protective as the sunscreen brand says they are.
Based on this evidence, I fully agree with the ban of non-mineral sunscreen because it is harmful to reefs and
the marine life within the reefs. It also impacts the fishing community, may result in less tourism income, and
lessen the beauty of the reefs throughout the islands of Hawaii. Non-mineral sunscreen may also harm human
health and cause water pollution. I hope this bill passes to fully ban sunscreen products that are harmful to the
reef. Thank you for giving your time and listening to my testimony, and aloha!

Aloha Council Chair Alice Lee, Vice-Chair Keani Rawlins-Femandez, and
Maui County Council Members. I am testifying in support of the Amendments to
Bill 118 (2021) relating to real property dedicated as ‘Aina Kupuna.
I would like to thank Vice-Chair Keani Rawlins-Fernandez for addressing the
long-standing issue of displacement on ‘aina kupuna by establishing a new
ordinance that provides tax relief Thank you also to the entire Maui County
Council for supporting the intent of this bill for our families in Maui Nui to remain
on our ‘aina ho‘oilina kupuna. We are grateful for the opportunities that this bill
will provide for our families maintaining and holding on to our ‘aina ho‘oilina
kupuna. Thank you to Council Chair Alice Lee for your leadership, long-standing
experience, and guidance bringing forth another momentous event in Maui County
for the betterment of our kama‘aina.
This bill gives hope and relief for my generation to continue the lifestyle, stories,
traditions, and beliefs of our parents, uncles and aunties, grandparents, and
ancestors who came before us. Being on our ‘aina kupuna brings us comfort in
times of grief, pain, confusion, happiness, joy, and celebration amongst our loved
ones and dear friends. The light shines upon us as we bear witness to an outcome
that we can celebrate with our kupuna who remain to see this day in person and in
spirit.

Dear Maui County Council
1 am writing to you today to express my support for Bill 135...
1,500 years ago, Polynesians moved onto Hawaii, and the reefs were beautiful and healthy.
Between 1994 and 2006, Hawaii lost 25% of its living coral, due to non reef safe sunscreen. If
this continues to go on, there is no denying that eventually all of Hawaii’s coral will die off,
and it will have a huge negative impact on the world. It will lead to a domino effect of mass
destruction. A huge amount of marine life will die due to their only source of food
disappearing.
Not to mention that if this happens, tourism will greatly decrease, because there are no
beautiful places in the ocean to go snorkeling in. Coral reefs protect coastlines from storms
and erosion. If there are no coral reefs left, Hawaii will be greatly more vulnerable to storms,
waves, and erosion. Over 200 million people rely on coral reefs to safeguard them from
storms. If coral reefs die, most of Hawaii's marine life will die and or move away and if that
happens, the fatalities can and will be huge on not only Hawaii but also the rest of the world.
And the government could stop this with a snap of  afinger, but to think they won’t just
because they want to make profits, it's cruel. The chemicals in sunscreen have already been
proven to damage and bleach coral reefs, so the real question is why? Why won’t our
lawmakers help save Hawaii?

TESTIMONY OF TINA YAMAKI, PRESIDENT
RETAIL MERCHANTS OF HAWAII
November 19, 2021
01‘- HIC E O F 1 H E
COUNTY CLERK
Re: Bill 135 (2021) A BILL FOR AN ORDINANCE ESTABLISHING CHAPTER 20.42, MAUI COUNTY CODE, TO
PROHIBIT THE SALE, USE, OR DISTRIBUTION OF NON-MINERAL SUNSCREENS
Good morning, Chair Lee, and members of the Maui County Council. 1 am Tina Yamaki, President of the Retail Merchants
of Hawaii and I appreciate this opportunity to testify.
The Retail Merchants of Hawaii was founded in 1901, RMH is a statewide, not for profit trade organization committed to
the growth and development of the retail industry in Hawaii. Our membership includes small mom & pop stores, large box
stores, resellers, luxury retail, department stores, shopping mails, local, national, and international retailers, chains, and
everyone in between.
We are OPPOSED to Bill 135 (2021) A Bill for An Ordinance Establishing Chapter 20.42, Maui County Code, To Prohibit
the Sale, Use, Or Distribution of Non-Mineral Sunscreens. Retailers continue to be concerned about our aina and have
supported many initiatives that preserve and protect not only our environment but or kupuna, keiki, residents and visitors
as well.
This measure goes way beyond the current state law that bans the sale and distribution of “oxybenzone and octinoxate" to
include any non-mineral sunscreen and would be the most restrictive sunscreen ban in the nation. It is our understanding
that over 64% of the current sunscreens that are currently sold in stores throughout the County of Maui will be taken off
the shelves in October 2022 when this measure would go into effect. This does NOT include the other forms sunscreen
that include but not limited to foundation makeup, lipsticks, lip balm, haircare products, insect repellents and
more.
Hawaii is known for its many sunny days and many residents and visitors who uses sunscreen include little
leaguers, hikers, golfers, soccer, baseball and tennis players, and joggers to name a few. With the pandemic we
are seeing more people and families enjoying outdoor by working in their yard, walking, shopping, biking, playing outside,
and going to the park. Sunscreens with zinc oxide or titanium dioxide that leave a white film on the skin where applied is
not what many in Hawaii want to use.
Many of us wear sunscreen daily to protect ourselves from the effects of the sun like skin cancer - the most
common form of cancer. Every year there are more cases of skin cancer in the United States than incidences of breast
cancer, prostate cancer, lung cancer, and colon cancer combined. One out of five Americans will develop skin cancer
in their lifetime, and one person dies of melanoma (the deadliest form of skin cancer) every hour. Most melanomas are
caused by the sun, and a person’s risk of melanoma doubles if he or she has had more than five sunburns. The Hawaii
Department of Health has also mentioned that is NOT advisable to limit sunscreens to only zinc/titanium as the active
ingredients from a skin cancer/human health perspective.
We may also run the risk of people no longer wearing sunscreen and thus increasing their chances of skin
cancer. We must consider that there a people who are very allergic or sensitive to the use of zinc oxide or titanium
dioxide and their sunscreen choices would be extremely limited if none at all. This ban on non-mineral sunscreens would
also penalize those who do not go to the beach but use sunscreen daily like hikers, golfers, tennis players and joggers to
name a few. Sunscreen products should be affordable and accessible as the first line of defense for individuals seeking
protection from the sun's cancer-causing UV rays. Banning the sale of non-mineral products will drastically reduce the
selection of sunscreen products available in Hawaii as well as compel local residents to purchase products online or not
use sunscreen at all and our visitors to bring their own in their suitcases.
We also wonder “How many people will actually take time off from work, pay a co-payment to see a doctor and
then wait in the pharmacy to a get their expensive prescription for sunscreen filled that most likely won’t be
covered by their health insurance?” The out-of-pocket cost would be the burden of the consumer as often time
insurance or prescription plans do not cover this cost.
3610 Waialae Ave ● Honolulu, HI 96816 - -- (808) 592-4200 “tyamaki@rmhawaii.org
Before acting on this measure, we feel the following questions needs to be addressed instead of assumptions.
● If pharmacies on Maui carry UV filter sunscreens, would they have to keep It behind the counter as this product would
now be “by prescription only”
● Is it within Maui County's jurisdiction to regulate prescription medications or over-the-counter drugs?
● Will health insurance companies recognize a prescription for sunscreen only for the County of Maui when the FDA
recognizes it as an over-the-counter drug?
● With Doctors and dermatologist prescribing sunscreen, are the people of Maui County willing to pay 2 to  3times more
for a sunscreen product that may not be covered by their health insurance?
If this bill passes, no one in Maui County will be able to use non-mineral sunscreen that you may have bought somewhere
else prior to this ban, online, or visitors who brought their own from their home with them to use on vacation. We also
wonder who would enforce this law. With tourist and local kamaaina traveling between islands and residents of Maui,
would there by a sunscreen police at the airport checking our bags or raiding our homes to confiscate our sunscreen, lip
balm, lipsticks, makeup foundation, haircare products, insect repellent and more.
While the FDA is studying chemical UV filters in sunscreens, it did NOT recommend banning them from use.
The FDA recommended no changes to currently marketed sunscreens. We ask that the Maui County Council to wait
until the National Academy of Science (NAS) completes their review scientific literature of sunscreen’s impact on
both environmental and human health. The NAS report should be published in Spring 2022 and will give decision
makers guidance on how to approach sunscreen rulings.
The solution of banning the use and sale of non-mineral sunscreen and other products is not the one-shot solution that
will solve the issues surrounding the coral reefs. Consumers, both visitors and kamaaina would have extremely limited
choices on sunscreen and products that are often less effective at blocking the sun and may cost a lot more. More
education and comprehensive studies of the coral reefs in their natural environment are needed. And we must be sure
that there are truly a large variety of effective sunscreen-based products that are easily available and are not cost
prohibitive.
We ask that you amend the bill to be in line with the state law banning only the sale and distribution of “oxybenzone and
octinoxate.
Mahalo for this opportunity to testify.
96816 I2i (808) 592-4200 ®3610 Waialae Ave ● Honolulu, HI tyamaki@rmhawaii.org

RE: Bill 135 to prohibit the sale, use, or distribution of non mineral sunscreens including those
containing oxybenzone, octinoxate, or both
Position: Oppose
The Hawaii Food Industry Association is comprised of two hundred member companies
representing retailers, suppliers, producers, and distributors of food and beverage related
products in the State of Hawaii.
The HFIA proposes that since this bill would ban many products that are used to prevent skin
cancer, that a higher standards of review should be conducted to ensure that taking this action
would indeed improve outcomes for reefs. The State of Hawaii recently conducted an
information review from top Hawaii scientist studying our reefs. The presenters discussed,
increased water temperatures, run-off, sewage and overfishing.
A study released on March 1,2018 on the human and natural impacts on coral reefs'* used 10
years worth of data to learn about what is negatively affecting our reefs. This study concluded
that overfishing, sedimentation, coastal development, and runoff are the biggest threats to
Hawaii’s corals. The study did not cite any sunscreen ingredients as posing dangers to our
reefs.
This measure will hurt local retailers by encouraging consumers to buy their favorite
sunscreens online, where it is unlikely this law will be enforceable. The promotion of this bill

Advancing the integration of spatial data to map human and natural drivers on coral reefs

will adversely impact human heath, serving only to deaminize wearing sunscreen, and
increase peoples risk of skin cancer.
We care about offering products individuals feel comfortable with and which are affordable for
use on a daily basis to prevent skin cancer. Many products that have sun protection factor,
such as lotions, tinted moisturizers, and anti-aging products are intended for daily use in small
amounts. These products are not used in large quantities anywhere near the ocean. However,
all of these products would be unnecessarily banned under this bill, as would other federally
approved and regulated healthcare products. Having access to these products is especially
important here in Hawaii where the rate of skin cancers, including deadly melanoma, is
significantly higher than on the mainland.^
Given that this ban would not alleviate the known primary causes of coral bleaching, and that it
would deprive people of products they use to prevent possibly life threatening skin cancers, we
do not think the potential benefit is worth the risk and we ask that this measure be held.
Thank you for the opportunity to testify.
